==PAGE:3==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Abstract
In recent years, sustainability has become a critical consideration in the construction in-
dustry, which significantly contributes to global warming through its reliance on steel and
concrete. Many civil engineering designs, particularly in geotechnical construction, are con-
servative due to inherent uncertainties. This study assesses the sustainability of the design
of a deep excavation by analyzing environmental, social, and economic dimensions for a ref-
erence and optimized design solution.
To achieve this, a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is conducted to evaluate the environmental
impact, complemented by an assessment of social factors and a budget analysis for the eco-
nomic dimension. The focus is on the design of a new metro station in Copenhagen, located
approximately 20 meters underground. The original deep excavation design was optimized
based on monitoring data, which showed improved responses compared to initial estimates.
Utilizing the widely recognized ReCiPe method for Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA),
it was discovered that the secant piles primarily influenced the design. Optimization efforts
targeted minimizing the thickness of these secant piles, resulting in the removal of one out
of two anchor levels and an increase in strut distance by 3.50 meters. This optimization led
to a reduction in environmental impact of 7.60-13.0% across all midpoint categories.
Furthermore, the simplified construction sequence improved social factors due to a reduction
in material usage, which also lowered the project budget. However, due to the limitations of
the methodologies employed, a comprehensive assessment of the social and economic pillars
could not be conducted.

Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
1
Introduction
As sustainability has become a crucial priority across various sectors, it has prompted coun-
tries, corporations, and industries to reevaluate their operational practices. The launch of
the United Nations’ 17 Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) in 2015 highlighted this shift,
with 193 member countries pledging to promote sustainable practices by 2030 [1]. Despite
its growing importance, the concept of sustainability remains complex and multifaceted, pre-
senting challenges in its definition, measurement, and practical application. The diversity
of interpretations, along with the absence of universally applicable standards, significantly
contributes to these complexities, making consistent definitions and assessments of sustain-
ability difficult. To aid in this process, several tools and methodologies have been developed
to evaluate the sustainable impacts associated with systems, processes, and products.
In practice engineers can contribute towards a more sustainable world with, among others,
more robust and descents designs, since engineers are used to almost every development,
meaning that they have a big influence on the future. However, these designs and products
still needs to be economically feasible and ensure the minimum amount of natural resources
and waste, along with providing social well-being. This leads to different challenges where
most of the problems are unique, difficult to formulate and several solutions exist but incom-
patible solutions are among, making the problems into wicked problems (which is problems
with many interdependent factors making them appear to be almost impossible to solve) [2].
Additionally, this does not change the fact that all engineers are in a situation where they
have an impact on the sustainable development.
Specifically, civil engineering plays a vital role in society, significantly influencing the out-
puts related to infrastructure, buildings, and transportation services. This encompasses not
only economic considerations but also the environmental and social impacts of construction
activities. As a key sub-discipline of civil engineering, geotechnical engineering directly con-
tributes to sustainable development, given its foundational role in the construction process.
Being one of the first link in the construction chain, geotechnical engineering is in an unique
positioned to incorporate sustainability principles during the early stages of project devel-
opment. However, the field still requires further exploration and innovation, as substantial
uncertainties and risks are often associated with the design processes. Consequently, ef-
forts to integrate sustainability into future designs must be approached carefully and with
thoughtful consideration.
In Denmark, there has been an increasing focus on implementing sustainability in geotech-
nical engineering. The Danish Geotechnical Society (Dansk Geoteknisk Forening, DGF) is
an association dedicated to fostering the sharing of geotechnical knowledge. Its mission is to
strengthen geotechnical expertise by gathering and highlighting established knowledge and
best practices. Recently, DGF held a seminar titled ’Climate Accounting in Geotechnics’ to
generate interest and disseminate information about the current state of geotechnical engi-
neering in Denmark [3].
The building and construction sector is also the largest contributor to greenhouse gas emis-
sions, accounting for approximately 37% of the global emissions [4]. This reality highlights
the urgent need to reduce dependence on heavy materials like concrete and steel. As the
1/ 83
==PAGE:8==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
sector seeks innovative sustainable practices, geotechnical engineering emerges as a critical
area that requires increased attention and research. Unfortunately, there is a scarcity of
sustainable methodologies specific to this field. Traditional design approaches often favor
conservative strategies, compounded by uncertainties related to soil conditions and hydraulic
factors, which pose significant challenges to sustainability assessments.
To address this gap, the present study focuses on optimizing the design of deep excavations.
These structures are commonly used for underground parking lots, tunnels, treatment plants,
pumping stations, foundations for bridges, and metro stations, among other applications. In
Denmark, it is common to use the stabilizing walls of deep excavations as temporary walls
rather than permanent ones. This practice leads to a significantly shorter lifecycle for the
walls compared to their intended use, as these walls will remain in the ground for the struc-
ture’s lifetime. This also includes other temporary structural designs needed for stabilizing
deep excavations, such as ground anchors.
Therefore, the aim of this study is to identify an optimized design solution for a deep exca-
vation associated with one of the new metro stations located at a depth of around 20 meters,
which was completed in 2024 in Copenhagen, Denmark.
Metroselskabet, the company responsible for the metro lines in Copenhagen, aims to reduce
its CO2 emissions by 50% across all aspects of its operations [5], indicating a focus on the
environmental impacts of its services.
However, this study goes beyond environmental considerations and examines how the de-
sign of deep excavations can be optimized for sustainability by incorporating environmental,
social, and economic factors. To validate whether the optimized design has improved sus-
tainability compared to the reference design, a final sustainability analysis will be presented.
With this a comprehensive sustainability assessment will be conducted with the aims to
identify critical factors that contribute to sustainability and to develop a comprehensive sus-
tainability assessment for deep excavations. Ultimately, this research aims to yield insights
that promote sustainable practices within the geotechnical field, contributing to the broader
goal of enhancing sustainability in the construction industry.
The challenges of defining sustainability lead to varying approaches for assessing sustain-
ability outcomes, especially given the absence of widely accepted standardized metrics. The
intention of this study is to develop a sustainability assessment based on the three pillars of
sustainability: environmental, social, and economical. This framework is a recognized ap-
proach for understanding sustainability, aiming to ensure that society has the opportunity
and capacity to thrive and advance without negatively impacting other critical factors [6].
Estimating these three pillars also lacks of a standardized method. Although several tools
are available to assess the pillars, some methods are more commonly used than others.
To find an optimized design solution for a deep excavation, a case study, outlined in section 3,
is needed for this research. Afterwards, a reference model will be established in section 4
using the same methodology employed in the original design of the metro station, specifically
through soil modeling. This approach ensures that the reference model accurately reflects
the performance observed during monitoring, which is essential for the design process. The
reference model is designed to resemble the metro station’s structure, enabling a comparison
of its behavior with the monitored data to validate its accuracy and effectiveness. In contrast,
2/ 83
==PAGE:9==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
an optimized model in the same chapter is also created, which incorporates enhancements
aimed at improving sustainability and operational efficiency. The obtained results for both
models are shown in section 5. By comparing the results between the reference model and
the optimized design, this study seeks to determine which approach yields better outcomes
in terms of performance and sustainability, which can be found in section 6.
The optimized design scenario will be based on the Eurocode 7: Geotechnical Design, EN1997
standard [7], focusing on geotechnical design and encompassing various verification limit
states. This methodology will facilitate the evaluation of sustainability impacts, such as
reductions in material and energy use. It is anticipated that future iterations of the EN1997
standard will incorporate an additional section dedicated specifically to sustainability, along-
side its current focus on Ultimate Limit State (ULS) and Serviceability Limit State (SLS)
considerations.
To assess the environmental pillar of this study, a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) will be con-
ducted. A description of what LCA entails is provided in section 2, while section 4 outlines
what the LCA entails in this study, including the goal definition, the inventory analysis, and
the method used for the Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA). More specifically, section 4
examines the processes from raw material extraction to the point where these materials are
placed in the deep excavation, adopting a cradle-to-site approach. The results will be pre-
sented in section 5 from both midpoint and endpoint perspectives, while section 6 discusses
the reference and optimized model results with each other and compares them with the lit-
erature.
The social pillar in section 4 will emphasize improvements in working conditions and exam-
ine how the optimized design reduces construction time. This enables users to access the
metro station more quickly, as detailed in section 5. The advantages and disadvantages of
the optimized design compared to the reference design concerning the social pillar will be
discussed in section 6.
The economic pillar will be thoroughly considered throughout the budgeting process de-
scribed in section 4, which encompasses the costs of materials, machinery, and operational
time. These results are presented in section 5, while the reference and optimized models are
discussed and compared in section 6.
At the end of the discussion in section 6, the sustainability assessment will take place,
highlighting the overall benefits of the optimized design. An overall performance assessment
will also be presented here, evaluating the three sustainability pillars and the soil modeling.
The study concludes with a summary of findings and recommendations for future research
in section 7.

2
Literature Review
In this section, different definitions of sustainability will be outlined, along with various
methods to measure and assess it. This section also highlights the importance of integrating
sustainability in the early phases, especially in relation to geotechnical engineering.
Sustainability is a widespread word where several definitions have been developed. However,
one popular phrase developed back in 1987 from the United Nations Brundtland Commission
defined sustainability as: ”meeting the needs of the present without compromising the ability
of future generations to meet their own needs” [8]. Another definition from IUCN (Interna-
tional Union for Conservation of Nature) states that sustainability refers to the enhancement
of human life quality while staying within the limits of the supporting ecosystem’s carrying
capacity [2]. Another recognized way to describe sustainability is with the three sustainabil-
ity pillars: economic, environmental and social [9]. While these three different definitions,
along with others, are recognized, they still do not explicitly outline a clear approach to
implementing sustainability in practice or addressing these issues [2].
With the many definitions of sustainability and its application as a term on both global
and local levels, measuring it becomes difficult due to the wide range of interpretations.
However, regardless of which definition is used or at what level development takes place, the
aim remains the same: to avoid negatively affecting future generations [10]. Nonetheless,
this leads to the typical challenge of implementing sustainability in practice, highlighting
that it is still a complex issue that complicates the search for new and better solutions,
which require further development overall. In the following section, the three sustainability
pillars will be described.
2.1
Three sustainable pillars
Achieving a world with more sustainable solutions, whether on a broader scale or through
specific projects, typically requires the support of the three key sustainable pillars. The goal
of these pillars is to ensure that society has the opportunity and capacity to thrive and ad-
vance without negatively impacting other critical factors [6]. To achieve these pillars, the 17
Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) provide a valuable framework for guidance. These
goals were adopted back in 2015, and they aim to achieve a more sustainable development
for humanity and the planet by 2030. The 193 member countries of the United Nations
are therefore obligated to achieve these goals [1]. The following sections will provide a brief
description of the three sustainability pillars in relations to the SDGs, specifically in relation
to geotechnical engineering.
Environmental
The environmental pillar, one of the most well-known aspects of sustainability, encompasses
issues such as climate change and the green transition. It emphasizes the importance of
protecting the climate, environment, and natural ecosystems [11]. While this pillar is often
equated with sustainability as a whole, it is essential to recognize that true sustainability
also incorporates social and economic dimensions.
4/ 83
==PAGE:11==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
For the environmental pillar SDG 9 is relevant for geotechnical engineering. SDG 9, ’Indus-
try, Innovation and Infrastructure’, focuses on improving infrastructure, where geotechnical
engineering plays a crucial role in creating sustainable and long-lasting structures. For in-
stance, it can facilitate economic growth and development by constructing such as tunnels
with enhanced longevity [1].
Social
Sustainability also focus on how to treat people equally and fairly which the social pillar aims
for [12]. Moreover, this pillar also addresses urban development, which encompasses several
aspects, including the provision of a safe environment and ensuring housing for everyone
[13]. For the social pillar is it also relevant to look ahead and predict future needs.
Civil engineering can contribute to the achievement of SDG 10, ’Reduced Inequality,’ by
creating designs that are accessible for everyone. This helps reducing social disparities and
promotes inclusive design, thereby supporting the goal of reducing inequality. Specifically,
geotechnical engineering can contribute to SDG 15, ’Life on Land,’ by ensuring that land use
changes are environmentally sustainable and safeguard ecosystems for future generations [1].
Economic
A healthy and stable economy in the long term is also important when considering sustain-
ability which the economic pillar consider. One essential thing regarding a stable economy
is to make financial decisions where short-term needs and long-term consequences are con-
sidered [11].
Geotechnical engineering is a broad field that contributes not only to the design but also
to the execution during the construction phase. Additionally, it often stimulates economic
growth, which aligns closely with the objectives of SDG 8, ’Decent Work and Economic
Growth.’
In addition, it is important to acknowledge that working conditions can vary
significantly depending on the project’s location. There is a growing global emphasis on
improving these conditions [1].
These three sustainability concepts encompass a wide range of expressions and methods,
leading to complex challenges that vary significantly based on the specific problem, location,
and the goals.
2.2
Geotechnical Practices for a Sustainable Future
Achieving sustainable development in geotechnical engineering requires a comprehensive con-
sideration of various factors, given that this discipline influences all three pillars of sustain-
ability outlined in the previous section. Similar to civil engineering, geotechnical engineering
is significantly dependent on the consumption of natural and energy resources, mainly from
steel and concrete production [14].
To address the challenges posed by this resource consumption, it is crucial to recognize the
role of site investigations in construction projects. These investigations help minimize re-
source use and enhance the effectiveness of sustainable practices. Soil properties can vary
significantly and rapidly, impacting design decisions. To account for these uncertainties, con-
servative designs are often adopted to mitigate associated risks. While some may criticize
5/ 83
==PAGE:12==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
these designs for potentially overestimating the use of materials like concrete and steel, fail-
ures in locations such as landfills can lead to severe environmental and social consequences,
including issues like leachate formation and the spread of contaminants. Thus, the pursuit
of sustainable designs necessitates the adoption of reliability-based approaches.
When building any structure, geotechnical engineering serves as the first link in the chain
regarding the construction site, indicating that this field has the potential to significantly
impact sustainable development. This can be achieved by helping to establish principles
and standards, particularly for minimizing environmental impacts at construction sites. As
illustrated in Figure 1, the ability to achieve highly sustainable solutions largely depends
on when sustainability practices are introduced. The earlier these principles are considered
in the project, especially during the design phase, the greater the sustainability impact.
However, as the project progresses from planning to execution, the availability of sustainable
alternatives decreases, making it essential for geotechnical engineers and other sectors to
integrate sustainability early in the design process.
Figure 1: Timeline of typical construction steps in geotechnical engineering, highlighting
optimal points for implementing sustainability [2]. All pictures are taken directly from the
following sites: [15], [16], [17], [18], and [19].
The impact of sustainability at an early stage largely depends on the owner, as the decision of
whether a structure needs to be sustainable typically falls beyond the scope of civil engineers.
A structure’s sustainability can be achieved in two ways from a geotechnical engineering
perspective, according to Dipanjan [2]: by doing the project right, or by doing the right
project.
In fact, choosing the right project can sometimes lead to greater success from
a sustainability perspective compared to merely following appropriate guidelines with good
engineering practices, which once again underline the complexity of sustainability. To obtain
the right project, several companies, have developed tools that can be used early in the
planning phase, allowing engineers and owners to discuss the relevance of sustainability at
an early phase.
This approach helps to gauge the clients’s sustainability ambitions and
incorporates the most suitable sustainable solutions to the specific project. It might also
help to reduce the significant barrier as cost and time which typically is the main focus,
leading to a more sustainable direction.
6/ 83
==PAGE:13==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
2.3
Sustainability assessment tools
To assess sustainability, various tools have been developed to address specific aspects. These
tools provide invaluable aids in understanding the sustainability performance of projects,
organizations, and products. Depending on the specific focus area within sustainability, dif-
ferent tools are available to measure the environment, social, and economic impacts. In the
following sections, different tools that are commonly used to evaluate sustainability within
each pillar is presented.
It is important to note that using any of the following tools will not constitute a sustain-
ability assessment. To achieve such an assessment, a tool that addresses all aspects must
be considered, or a combination of several tools that represent each pillar could be used,
possibly through a sustainability index. A sustainability assessment can ultimately lead to
the best sustainability solution, depending on the goal.
2.3.1
Environmental tools
To obtain a sustainable assessment, one common way to evaluate the environmental pil-
lar is to perform a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) which follows the guidelines specified in
ISO 14040 [20], the ”Environmental management - Life cycle assessment - Principles and
framework” and ISO 14044 [21], the ”Environmental management - Life cycle assessment -
Requirements and guidelines”.
This tool is used to evaluate the environmental impacts of a service, process, or product
throughout its entire life cycle. This includes the raw materials, production, use, and post-
use phases. By performing an LCA, various benefits can be achieved. This includes improved
environmental communication, product development, and evidence-based policy making, de-
pending on the purpose of the LCA.
A typical LCA consists of four stages, Goal and Scope Definition, Life Cycle Inventory,
Impact Assessment, and Results and Interpretation, these phases will be described in the
following.
Goal and Scope Fefinition
This phase marks the initial stage of the LCA, encompassing the definitions of both the goal
and the scope. This includes primarily the intended application, the reasons for applying
the study, the system boundary, and the functional unit. By presenting these aspects of the
LCA first, it will be easier to use and interpret the obtained results in the end.
Since this study focuses on deep excavations, it is necessary to establish a system boundary
for a clearer understanding of such type of analysis. Figure 2 outlines the various types of
system boundaries in general for LCAs and illustrates the specific stages of deep construction
for its life cycle. By defining these system boundaries, the LCA aims to evaluate and compare
the environmental impacts associated with raw material use and the production processes
involved in deep excavation construction.
7/ 83
==PAGE:14==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Figure 2: Links between the stages in a deep excavation life cycle and the system boundaries
related to LCA.
One crucial aspect of conducting an LCA is determining a relevant functional unit, FU, this
unit is an important parameter for the LCA. It makes it possible to compare LCA results
with each other, which is important when different systems or products has to be assessed,
here the functional unit helps to make sure that the comparisons are made on a common
basis. To do so the FU is done by making a reference of the inputs and outputs [20].
Since no standard has been defined for the functional unit, each assessment must establish
its own. Consequently, it becomes challenging to compare new results with previous as-
sessments and hinder further development of LCA for addressing specific problems. Several
studies have been conducted in the field of geotechnical engineering, but errors still persist.
For example, in Pons, 2018 [22], an LCA examined five different earth-retaining wall types.
The FU in this study was defined as ”a linear meter of wall for a determined height”. In
another study, Damians, 2020 [23], four different wall types with a height of 5 meters were
investigated. In that case, the FU was defined as: ”for each one meter-width of wall face
and assuming a wall height of 5 m, horizontal back slope and design life of 100 years”. While
this functional unit provides a clear framework for its analysis, it is notably specific due to
the requirement of a fixed wall height of five meters. This is very specific and may limit
the applicability of the study’s findings to other contexts, as many other studies may utilize
different wall heights or configurations for comparison. Therefore, utilizing a more stan-
dardized or adaptable functional unit, such as a per running meter, would likely enhance the
comparability and overall utility of the study’s results. However, other LCA studies related
to geotechnical engineering often fail to specify the FU (e.g., Misra 2012 [14] and Liu (2020)
[24]).
8/ 83
==PAGE:15==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Inventory analysis
The Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) phase involves data collection, where the environmental in-
puts and outputs associated with the service, product, or process are considered. Inputs
can include raw materials, which are extracted from the environment to use in the product.
Additionally, outputs can include emissions of pollutants from the production.
Several LCI databases have been sourced and serve as the secondary data for the LCA, pro-
viding scientific average data that facilitates the LCA process without requiring raw primary
data. The largest LCI database is the EcoInvent database, which is the most transparent
database available on the market [25]. This database covers a diverse range of sectors and
includes more than 20.0 thousand datasets related to human activities and processes. Its
purpose is to enhance the understanding of the environmental impacts of the products and
services being used [26].
However, a disadvantage of this database is the large number
of datasets, which can lead to uncertainties when trying to identify the most appropriate
dataset.
Impact assessment
Once the correct data is obtained in the previous phase, the Life Cycle Impact Assessment
(LCIA) can begin. In this phase, the environmental impacts are translated into environmen-
tal themes, such as global warming by using different methods. This allows it to understand
the sustainability challenges associated with the assessed system.
There are different methodologies that can be used in the LCIA phase, however, methods
like ReCiPe and IPCC 2013 are among the most widely used for conducting the LCIA [27].
The ReCiPe method contains of a midpoint and endpoint analsyis. The midpoint anal-
ysis quantifies environmental impacts more directly, focusing on specific effects related to
emissions and adopting a problem oriented approach. In contrast, the endpoint method
emphasizes long-term consequences, highlighting damage to human health, resource avail-
ability, and ecosystem integrity, which represent the broader, long-term impacts of emissions
[28].
When utilizing the ReCiPe 2016 method, the midpoint analysis encompasses 18 impact cat-
egories to address a wide variety of effects, all of which are detailed in Appendix B.1. The
endpoint analysis integrates these 18 categories along with damage factors, which are mul-
tiplied together and aggregated into three endpoint categories: human health, ecosystems,
and resource availability. An overview of this process is illustrated in Figure 3. The char-
acterization factors are representative of a global scale, with normalization factors derived
from statistics for the reference year 2010 [29].
9/ 83
==PAGE:16==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Figure 3: Relationships between the midpoint impact categories and the endpoint areas.
When considering the endpoint method the units for the impact categories are expressed
in common units. Considering human health, the unit used is ’DALY’ (Disability-Adjusted
Life Years), which takes into account the years people live with disabilities and the years
lost. This unit is also used by the World Health Organization (WHO) and the World Bank.
Regarding resources, the unit used is ’USD2013’, which represents the surplus costs of future
resource production over an infinite time frame. The last category, ecosystem, is measured
in ’species.yr’, which describes the loss of species over a certain area during a certain time [30].
Results & Interpretation
The final phase of the LCA involves results and interpretation. In this step, the results
are presented along with the interpretations and conclusions drawn from the assessment.
Additionally, any comparisons with other assessments, if applicable, are made [31].
To perform an LCA, different tools have been developed, and some of these software will be
described below.
10/ 83
==PAGE:17==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Different tools
Table 1 shows three different LCA tools, all of which use ISO 14044/14040 [20] [21]. It is
important to note that an LCA requires multiple impact categories so that the trade-offs
among environmental impacts can be considered. This means that if the calculation only
provides a single impact category (such as global warming potential (GWP)), it is a footprint
analysis rather than an LCA. For footprint assessments, the standard PAS 2050:2011 is one
of the applications [32]. Table 2 presents two tools that focus on footprints, which are still
valuable to consider when exploring more sustainable designs in a geotechnical context. Both
tools in Table 2 focus on the building and geotechnical sectors, meaning the used databases
are specific to these areas. In contrast, SimaPro and OpenLCA, shown in Table 1, are not
sector-specific, resulting in very broad databases where errors in data selection might occur,
such as selecting the wrong process. The tools represented in Table 1, are also more complex
compared to the tools in Table 2.

Even though the LCA is a popular method, there is still a lack of information regarding its
applications in geotechnical engineering. This lack of information is due to several factors.
One key issue is that there are no specific standards for LCA in geotechnical engineering,
leading researchers in different directions and causing the same mistakes to be repeated.
The ISO 14040 also states that no single method is prescribed for conducting an LCA. This
allows organizations to implement their own LCA, still based on ISO 14040/44, but with
the flexibility to adapt the organization’s specific criteria depending on the aim of the LCA
[20].
Vejdirektoratet in Denmark, a public institution operating under the Ministry of Transport,
is one of the organizations that has developed its own LCA tool. These types of LCAs
creates a database that is very narrow and precise for the organization’s specific needs,
allowing Vejdirektoratet to focus on what is relevant for the Danish context within this
sector. By using its own tool, a better understanding of the environmental results can be
achieved, making it easier to implement sustainable solutions in this specific sector [33].
2.3.2
Social tools
Incorporating social tools into sustainability assessments is vital for understanding and man-
aging the social impacts of construction projects on local communities. Furthermore, it is
important to note that measuring social aspects presents more challenges than measuring
environmental ones. To effectively monitor these social impacts, a comprehensive approach
could involve administering a questionnaire to residents living close to the construction site.
This questionnaire could be conducted prior to, during, and after the construction period,
allowing for ongoing interaction and engagement with the local community. This approach
seeks to ensure that the concerns and opinions of the population are actively acknowledged
and addressed, fostering a sense of inclusivity and participation.
12/ 83
==PAGE:19==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Another social tool could be the performance of a Social Life Cycle Assessment (SLCA),
which aim to measure the impact of a process or product on society throughout the full life
cycle. This assessment is similar to the LCA, which also follows the four steps described
earlier. However, this assessment focuses more on the social impact on people [34].
Assessing the social impacts from a stakeholder’s perspective is also relevant. The Social
Impact Assessment (SIA) tool facilitates this evaluation by assessing processes to ensure
that stakeholder projects align with societal concerns and needs. The tool helps identifying
both positive and negative consequences while also aiming to prevent adverse effects before
they occur. Other than that it also contributes to establishing positive relationships with the
community and garnering support for the project. Ultimately, SIA promotes corporate social
value and enhances citizenship. By utilizing this tool, organizations can better understand
the impact of their own actions on the surroundings and the environment [35].
2.3.3
Economic tools
One way to incorporate the economic pillar is by conducting a Life-Cycle Cost Analysis
(LCCA). This method aims to estimate the overall costs of a project alternatives, compar-
ing designs to determine the most cost-effective option in terms of function and quality when
the performance requirements are the same. The LCCA takes into account ownership, dis-
posal, and acquisition costs of the building system.
To select the design alternative with the lowest overall cost, the analysis should be conducted
during the early design process. However, this task is challenging as it requires evaluating
the economic effects of future buildings and expressing them in monetary terms [36].
Another tool to consider is the Cost-Benefit Analysis (CBA), which is performed over a
specific time period rather than the entire lifetime as the LCCA. The CBA does not only
consider the anticipated costs but also examines the benefits associated with different solu-
tions. Through this analysis, decisions can be made by assessing the total expected outcomes
from a particular action. To determine the project’s worthiness, the associated costs are
subtracted from the benefits. If the benefits outweigh the costs, the decision is considered
favorable and worthwhile [37].
2.4
Soil Modeling
Ensuring the stability of geotechnical engineering designs is critical for the success of a specific
project. In this context, the design must meet the requirements of Eurocode 7: Geotechnical
Design, EN 1997 standard [7]. Numerical modeling can be an essential tool for addressing
complex geotechnical challenges, allowing for computational simulations of various scenarios.
The numerical model typically employs mathematical representations to describe physical
relationships within the selected scenarios through equations and numerical data.
Plaxis 2D is one of the software programs designed to analyze various geotechnical compo-
nents. This finite element software effectively analyzes stability, deformation, and ground-
water flow in geotechnical engineering. For a comprehensive understanding of the software,
please refer to its manual [38].
13/ 83
==PAGE:20==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
The cross-section of interest will be modeled in Plaxis 2D, this includes a borehole to include
the necessary soil layers and their characteristic parameters.
Afterwards the structures will be modeled in the geometry, where the walls will be modeled
with an interface, along with the required anchors or struts. To model the soil-structure
interaction properly, the interface is important, as it helps to simulate how the soil behaves
along the plate. The surface load will also be applied with the specified parameters. After
the geometry has been set, the calculation set up can start. To obtain the most accurate
results, the mesh needs to be sufficiently fine. However, it is important that the mesh is not
too fine, as this can result in excessive calculation time. The mesh in Plaxis 2D is generated
automatically through a robust triangulation procedure that considers the soil stratigra-
phy, structural objects, loads, and boundary conditions [39]. Additionally, the groundwater
conditions will be defined, along with the staged construction sequence.

January, 2025

3.1

When designing the metro station, five different load cases were considered during its design,
outlined in Table 4. For this study, ULS LC2 will be used. This load case occupies a middle
position between the different load cases, which leads to a balanced approach in the design,

assessment that reflects the expected loads and soil conditions in reality.

surface load and the building load from Fisketorvet include safety factors, contributing to a
more robust design that can support potential dynamic and static forces. This consideration
is crucial given the project’s location in a densely populated area.

one. However, it is important to acknowledge that this sequence may be incorrect, and errors
in the output can be anticipated. Only the construction sequences which is used for this

3.3.1

tests were performed, encompassing point load tests on limestone, triaxial tests on clay till
and limestone, and oedometer tests on clay till [41]. Out of the 18 boreholes, only two were
located within the construction pit, while the remaining boreholes were positioned around
the site, as illustrated in the drawing ’Plan and Geotechnical Longitudinal Section’ [47].

3.3.2
Hydrogeologic conditions
Long-term groundwater level monitoring was conducted for one of the two boreholes placed
inside the excavation pit, focusing on the primary aquifer, from August 2015 to July 2018.
Throughout this period, fluctuations in groundwater levels were noted, influenced by sewage
drainage, precipitation, and nearby harbor water levels. These fluctuations corresponded
with harbor water levels from August 2015 to March 2017, with recorded groundwater levels
ranging from +0.55 to -2.18 m DVR90 [41].
3.4
Monitoring
During the construction of the metro station at Havneholmen, various actions and proce-
dures were undertaken to monitor the construction work and its effects on nearby buildings
and surroundings. Both manual and automatic monitoring methods were employed, and all
the collected data was stored in the Kronos database. Kronos is a database that gathers the
20/ 83
==PAGE:27==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
monitoring data from all the metro stations in Sydhavnen, ensuring centralized and compre-
hensive monitoring of the construction activities and their impact on the surrounding area.
In this section, a brief list of the different monitoring systems will be provided.
Monitoring on Structural Stability
Four types of monitoring tests were conducted on the retaining wall. Eight inclinometers
were installed at pre-excavation, featuring 3-meter sensors. After excavation, the sensors
were removed, and measurements were taken manually. Load cells were placed on 10% of
the anchors. For the tubular struts, 50% were equipped with strain gauges, ideally with
three gauges per side. All measurements were recorded automatically every four hours, and
transmitted to the Kronos database. The measurements was increased to every two hours if
alert limits were reached to ensure timely detection of any anomalies.
Surroundings
To prevent potential damage to existing buildings during excavation, monitoring of exposed
structures was essential. An investigation identified settlement zones, generating curves for
settlements measurements of 1.00, 2.00, 5.00, 10.0, 15.0, and 20.0 mm (see Figure 3 in Mon-
itoring Plan HOL report [44]). Buildings within the five millimeters settlement zone and
sensitive buildings within the two millimeters zone required monitoring. Specifically, two
buildings and a wall ramp were monitored manually using precise leveling, to make sure no
settlement occurred.
Ground Water and Sea Level
As mentioned previously, two aquifers was present at the site, prompting the establishment
of two monitoring wells to track groundwater levels and flow conditions through both man-
ual and automatic monitoring. Additionally, groundwater levels near the retaining walls
require close monitoring, especially during fluctuations, which is addressed by installing two
piezometers. Each piezometer features shallow and deep standpipes to collect data at various
depths, ensuring a comprehensive understanding of the site’s groundwater conditions [44].
By utilizing these different types of monitoring, the expected behavior could be investigated.
This was demonstrated in a Technical Report [45], which compared the predicted behaviors
with the observed behaviors recorded from the monitoring system of the temporary retaining
wall. The report highlights the differences between the detailed design values and the as-
built values, and all interpretations in this context are based directly on the findings from the
report. Notably, it reveals that the stiffness of the second level of anchors was, on average,
21% greater in the as-built condition compared to the detailed design report. Additionally,
the horizontal displacement for Stage 9 from the construction sequence outlined in subsec-
tion 3.2 differed by -13.0 mm when comparing design values to Inclinometer six, which was
located closest to Fisketorvet Mall. These findings from the Technical Report suggest that
such types of construction are often overestimated during the design phase, reinforcing the
importance of this study.
21/ 83
==PAGE:28==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
4
Methodology
In this section, the soil modeling setup for both the reference and optimized models will be
described, utilizing Plaxis 2D as the software tool. Relevant inputs and methods required
for the analysis will be detailed in the following section.
Additionally, the first three phases of the Life Cycle Assessment will be outlined in detail.
Afterwards, the social pillar will cover the elements that influence social aspects, while the
economic pillar will present the budget outline.

4.1.1
Input Parameters for Reference Model
To achieve the most realistic model the stiffness (elastic axial rigidity, EA) and the pre-
stressed values for the two anchor levels are aligned with the detailed design presented in the
Structural Analysis Report, and shown in Table 6. Each anchor is placed every 1.80 meters,
while the remaining dimensions of the anchors are presented in section 3.
For the strut, which is placed every 6.50 meters, a CHS 1016x20 profile is used at the
examined cross-section [42]. This profile has a sectional area of 626 cm2 [48], and its stiffness
is assumed to be 210 GPa, as no additional information is provided. The elastic axial rigidity
of the strut is also shown in Table 6.
Regarding the secant piles, concrete type C30/37 is utilized, and the intact flexural stiffness,
EI, is set at 3.55·106 kNm2/m. The stiffness of the wall is also shown in Table 6.

The soil parameters utilized in this study follow the design stratigraphy previously described
(refer to Table 5) and adopt Mohr-Coulomb drained conditions. However, using this model
in Plaxis 2D introduces some uncertainties, as it only considers normally consolidated soil,
which may not be representative of some areas in Denmark due to the country’s glacial
history. Consequently, the at rest coefficient, K0, must be set manually, for the ULS LC2,
followed by the inputs for the FREW model detailed in Appendix 10 of the Structural Anal-
ysis Report [42]. This information is presented in Table 53 in Appendix C. For the SLS case
the lower bound values for K0 in Table 5 is used. Upper and lower bound values for the Rock
Mass Young’s Modulus is also outlined in the table, where both cases uses the lower bound
values. SLS and ULS LC2 uses a interphase equal 0.67 for all soil layers which has also been
23/ 83
==PAGE:30==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
adopted for this model, this is done to get the most representative model. Tension cut-off is
applied for all soil layers except the fill layer, the values are shown in Table 53 in Appendix
C. Since no specific information is provided regarding the modeling of groundwater, standard
values for permeability are applied in the Plaxis 2D model.
The following figure shows the cross-section of the reference Plaxis 2D model, where it is
seen that the strut is modeled as a Fixed-end anchor. Additionally, the strut is pre-stressed
to 100 kN in the model due to numerical reasons. The free lengths of the anchors are mod-
eled as Node-to-node anchors, while the fixed lengths are represented as an Embedded beam
row. The wall is modeled as a Plate, with a positive and negative interface. The interface
is modeled along the wall, however, it extends one meter below the wall to account for the
soil-structure interaction beneath it. Both the anchors, struts, and wall are all modeled with
elastic properties.
The water table is initially modeled at 0.00 mDVR90 and decreases to 0.50 meters below
the ground surface within the construction pit, while remaining at its initial level outside
the pit throughout all stages.
Once everything is set up, the mesh can be generated. The connectivity plot for the reference
model is shown in Figure 14.

Since the design is different from the reference model the construction sequence is also
difference, the new sequence is listed below:
1. Initial conditions.
2. Surface preparation, construction of the secant pile guide-wall, installation of the secant
pile, and installation of the groundwater control and station dewatering system.
3. Excavation to -3.50 m and internal groundwater lowering.
4. Anchor installation at Level 1, -2.00 m.
5. Excavation to -13.0 m and internal groundwater lowering.
6. Installation of temporary steel strutting at Level 2, -12.0 m.
7. Excavation to -20.8 m and internal groundwater lowering.
The mesh for the optimized model is generated in the same manner as that of the reference
model. Figure 68 in Appendix C.2.1 shows the connectivity plot for the optimized model.
4.2
Reinforcement Design
The secondary secant piles contain reinforcement cages, where the necessary design is imple-
mented. The piles incorporate both shear and flexural reinforcement, and the calculations
are shown in Appendix D, following the requirements outlined in Eurocode 2. The calcula-
tions for reinforcement also adhere to the methodology described in the Structural Analysis
Report (refer to Appendix 4 in Structural Anal-ysis Report [42]) to ensure transparent
calculations. Importantly, the reinforcement calculations aim to achieve the same level of
utilization and the same column sections as reported in the Structural Analysis Report,
thereby ensuring transparency in the results. It is essential to have transparent calculations
to later make accurate comparisons between the reference and optimized models, as relying
solely on the results from the Structural Analysis Report would be inadequate in this context.
Going from the reference design to the optimized design, the concrete cover must be changed
to comply with the structural requirements outlined in Eurocode 2. It is known that the
concrete cover used for Havneholmen is 90.0 mm, which is a conservative approach, consid-
ering that Eurocode 2 only mandates a concrete cover of 40.0 mm [49]. Conversely, HOFOR
requires a concrete cover of 90.0 mm for their permanent secant piles, while not specifying
requirements for temporary secant piles [50]. This suggests that there may be flexibility in
reducing the concrete cover safely during temporary design, resulting in the optimized design
having a concrete cover set to 40.0 mm.
26/ 83
==PAGE:33==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
4.3
Goal and Scope Definition of Environmental Assessment
In the following the first phase of the LCA will be described, which includes the goal and
scope definition.
4.3.1
Goal Definition
The goal of this LCA study is to quantify the environmental impact by optimizing the design
of a deep excavation associated with the expansion of the Metro in Sydhavnen, Copenhagen.
This is done in order to identify critical areas for reducing CO2 emissions by 50%, which
aligns with Metroselskabet’s overall objective of minimizing the environmental impact across
all aspects of their operations [5].
Additionally, the study aims to gain insight into the current environmental impact of geotech-
nical processes involved in deep excavations through hotspot analysis. While this study will
not drive significant changes in the execution of deep excavations, it can potentially be uti-
lized in the future to enhance design optimization.
The LCA study is conducted as a comparative analysis, with the goal of defining the optimal
design solution. To achieve this, the process with the highest contribution to the overall
environmental impact within the product system will be identified.
4.3.2
Scope Definition
Figure 2 shows different boundary conditions typically used. For this study, the ’cradle-to-
site’ approach is employed, which encompasses the stages from raw materials to the con-
struction process.
For a more specific system boundary Figure 16 is needed. This figure include a series of steps
that are typically involved in deep excavation projects. Although the specific procedures may
vary depending on the design. The foreground data, marked in blue in Figure 16, refers to
specific, measurable inputs and processes directly related to the construction project, such as
the energy consumption and emissions generated during deep construction activities like wall
stabilization. In contrast, background data (marked with green in the figure) encompasses
broader information. This includes the environmental impacts associated with, for exam-
ple, cement production and transportation to the site for constructing the wall. This data
provides essential context for understanding the overall sustainability of the construction
process.
27/ 83
==PAGE:34==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Figure 16: System boundaries for the deep excavation.
Function and the Functional Unit
Throughout the research project, two designs for a deep excavation need to be performed,
meaning that the function and functional unit must accommodate both designs. The func-
tion for this study is to stabilize a deep excavation, while the functional unit is defined as
”Stabilizing a deep excavation of 1 m depth pr. m2 area for 100 years according to stan-
dard EN1997”. This functional unit ensures that the focus is only on deep excavations,
which are defined as any excavation more than 4.5 meters in depth [51]. Additionally, the
functional unit also considers Eurocode 7 for geotechnical designs [7]. The duration is set to
100 years based on other functional units described in the literature (see subsubsection 2.3.1).
This functional unit ensures the ability to uniformly compare all the different processes. By
using a broad functional unit like this, the method remains flexible and robust enough to
cover various solutions without changing the overall unit of measurement. This approach
makes it possible to evaluate the different environmental impacts in a standardized way.
4.4
Inventory Analysis of Environmental Assessment
The Inventory analysis is the third phase of the LCA. The following sections will contain all
the needed information when looking into the reference model along with the data collection.
Regarding the optimized model the analysis is the same as described in the following, unless
otherwise stated in subsubsection 4.4.5.

the analysis due to the lack of relevant documentation and data. The processes within the

this assessment, obtaining first-hand data from the Kronos database, along with inputs from

while the processes taken from the EcoInvent Database are regarded as secondary data.

sentativeness are indicated. High representativeness in the table suggests that the data are

from the Kronos database, design reports, or Metroselskabet itself. Conversely, when a pro-
cess or subprocess has low representativeness, it indicates that either little or poor-quality
data has been provided, necessitating numerous assumptions. Typically, the specifications
from the EcoInvent database demonstrate one level of representativeness lower than those
sourced mainly from the Kronos or design reports. A more detailed specification of the
subprocesses and their representativeness is provided in Appendix A.6.

Transport
There is no information on the type of lorry used for transportation of the materials to and
from the construction site. Therefore, it is assumed that the lorries weigh over 32 metric
tons and comply with the EURO5 emissions standard, resulting in ’Transport, freight, lorry
> 32 metric ton, EURO5, RER, market for transport’ from the EcoInvent database.
While EURO6 is considered as the best emissions standard, most lorries do not meet its
requirements due to their longer lifespan and the fact that older vehicles typically do not
conform to the EURO6 standard [56].
It is important to note that transportation is considered from the manufacturer of the ma-
terials rather than the supplier when that information is available. This often results in a
longer distance when considering the materials from the Kronos database. Consequently,
this leads to conservative estimates for transportation, which may offset some of the uncer-
tainties related to machine operation.

Information regarding the cement to the secant piles are found in two different reports named:
”Notification of Material (NOM)” [57] [58]. These different cement types follows respectively
the primary and secondary piles. The primary piles contain cement with C25/20 designation,
and the density is 2208 kg/m3, while the secondary piles contains cement with a C30/37
designation, with a density of 2237 kg/m3. The amount of secant piles is registered in the
Kronos database, which is equal to 338 secant piles. Among these piles, 27 are classified as
soft piles, while 25 are unnamed (see Appendix A.1 for details). The unnamed piles, without
reinforcement, are regarded as primary piles, whereas the reinforced piles are categorized as
secondary piles. Table 9 summarizes the quantities of the two types of secant piles, along

Anchors
It is known that there are a total of 120 anchors, with 30 anchors at each of the two levels
on each side [44]. Each anchor contains 11 wires made of steel type S355 [42]. The weight of
one wire is 4.00 kg/m [66], resulting in a total weight of 100 tonnes for all the used wires, see
Appendix A.3 for detail calculations. It is important to note that the EcoInvent database
does not include steel type S355. Therefore, ’Reinforcing steel, GLO, market for reinforcing
steel’ is used as an approximation for the wires.
The grout in the anchor is connected to the fixed length, which is eight or nine meters de-
pending on the anchor level. Additionally, the diameter of the grouted section is given as
187 mm [42], resulting in 26.5 m3 of needed grout in total, see Appendix A.3 for calculation.
It is also given that the quality for the grout is C50/60 [67], leading to a density equal 2316
kg/m3 [68]. The used process from the EcoInvent databased is ’Concrete, 50 MPa, RoW,
market for concrete, 50 MPa’.
To distribute the load from the wires, the anchor also requires steel plates. The dimensions of
these steel plates can be found in the ”Notification of Material (NOM)” [69], where the total
volume of one plate is 1.18·10−2 m3. With the bore diameter subtracted, the total weight of
one anchor plate is therefore 92.8 kg, see Table 43 in Appendix A.2 for details. The anchor
plates are considered as ’Hot rolling, steel, GLO, market for hot rolling, steel’, which appears
as a best fit due to the anchor plates needs to be formed in shape. The manufacturer of the
steel plates is located in Dortmund, resulting in 669 km of transport [69], while the manu-
facturer of the wires is in Austria, necessitating a transportation distance of 1.13·103 km [70].

Waler Beam and Headwall Beam
It is stated in the Structural Analysis Report [42] that the waler and headwall beams are
used on the same level as the struts, which is expected (see Table 11). It is assessed that
these two types of beams are identical but are referred to by different names to reflect their
specific roles during the design and construction. In each level, 2HEB1000 beams are used
for both the waler beam and the headwall beam. This type of HEB beam has a weight of
314 kg/m [72]. These beams are most likely connected to all the piles, resulting in an inner
length of 16.8 m and a width of 66.3 m. This results in a total weight of 626 tonnes for both
the waler and headwall beams, the calculations are shown in Table 40 in Appendix A.2. In
the EcoInvent database, these beams are referred to by the same name as the struts.
As the struts and beams serve as a temporary design, they will be removed from the con-
struction site and will not last for 100 years, as the functional unit states. Therefore, these
elements are also considered waste. Along with the EcoInvent database, it is expected that
for every 1 kg of struts and beams, 28% will end up at a sorting plant, 62% will be recycled,
and 10% will be collected for final disposal. However, it must be noted that due to limita-
tions, these elements are not considered suitable for reuse for the same purpose because of
insufficient information from the construction site. It is assumed that these materials will
be disposed of for other uses or remelted for different applications.

Fasteners and connectors
To facilitate the construction of the deep excavation, an extensive range of components such
as bolts, weldings, and various types of plates are required. However, it is important to note
that due to incomplete information, there may be certain discrepancies in this part of the
data collection. Despite the possibility of missing data, it has been assessed that the absence
of these components will not significantly impact the final result.
Upon examining the welding requirements for the construction of the struts and waler beams,
it has been found that a total of 15 meters of welding is needed, calculations are shown in
Table 44 in Appendix A.2 obtained from the three different Structural Analysis Report Hol
– Strutting/Steel Design Bracing documents [67] [73] [74]. ’Welding, arc, steel, GLO, market
for welding, arc, steel’ is used from the EcoIvent database.
Furthermore, approximately 3800 bolts are assessed to be necessary for the structure, result-
ing in a total weight of 2.80 · 103 kg for the required steel bolts. See Table 45 in Appendix
A.2 for detailed calculations. From the database the belonging process is ’Steel, low-alloyed,
hot rolled, GLO, market for steel, low-alloyed, hot rolled’. Nevertheless, it has also been
anticipated that the impact of these processes on the final outcome is not significant, and
therefore, the quantities assessed are still considered sufficient.
By taking these factors into consideration, it can be concluded that although there may be
some minor data gaps, the overall construction progress and the final results will not be
significantly affected by this.
Groundwater control
Before starting the excavation, groundwater control is necessary. According to the Kronos
database, the number of AW pumps is 12, and there are 19 RW pumps. AW pumps are
responsible for removing water from the construction pit, while RW pumps stabilize the
surrounding water. The database does not specify details about these pumps, leading to the
assumption that each pump consumes 22.0 kW [75]. Due to this lack of information, the
only suitable process in the EcoInvent database is ’Water pump, 22 kW, GLO, market for
water pump,’ where the number of pumps is the only required parameter.
According to the Kronos database, the pumps have been operating for approximately three
years, and water flow measurements have been taken every 15 minutes. The total amount of
water pumped by the RW pumps is 4.28·105 m3, and 17.7·105 m3 for the AW pumps, details
are shown in Table 36 in Appendix A.1.
34/ 83
==PAGE:41==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Excavated soil/limestone
Regarding soil disposal, a total excavation volume of 3.20·104 m3 is considered, as detailed in
Table 38 in Appendix A.2. The excavation is overall divided into soil and limestone layers,
with specific volumes detailed in Table 39 in Appendix A.2. The soil layer starts at +1.90
mDVR90 and extends to -7.50 mDVR90, followed by the limestone layer, which continues
until the end of the excavation pit, as indicated in Table 5. The swelling factor is applicable
to both categories, with a factor of 1.20 for the soil and 1.70 for the limestone [76]. This
results in a total soil volume of 1.58·104 m3 and the limestone is equal to 2.68·104 m3. For
further calculations, please refer to Table 39 in Appendix A.1.
The disposal of excavated soil, is assumed to be located 22.0 km away from the construction
site. This assumption is based on the proximity of Østedværket (ØVK), which is situated
20.0 km from the disposal site and only 2.00 km from the Havneholmen station. This as-
sumption is made together with Metroselskabet.
In the excavation process, distinct types of excavators are required: soil excavators for soil
materials and rock breakers for rock materials. For this study, it is assumed that four soil
excavators were used, and two rock breakers were employed once the limestone layer was
reached.
Waste/Diposal
The Kronos database includes monthly waste reports, summarized in Table 34 in Appendix
A.1 for the Havneholmen station. When modeling waste in the SimaPro software, it is crucial
that the process is precise to achieve accurate results. Nevertheless, the waste reports lack
detailed information, offering only the fractions without additional specifics. As a result,
the processes selected from the EcoInvent database are deemed sufficient given the volume
of the waste.
4.4.3
Unit Processes
For modeling the relevant processes in the software SimaPro, it was necessary to create a
process unit for each modeled process. In this assessment, each process collected in SimaPro
was the best fit in relation to the primary data and was collected under ’inputs from tech-
nosphere: materials/fuels’, which describe the materials and resources that are added to the
unit process. Details of each unit process can be found in Appendix A.7.
Figure 40 in Appendix A.7 indicates the unit process for the struts and beams. This figure
also shows a process under ’outputs to technosphere: Waste and emissions to treatment’,
which is also the case for the unit process of Waste in Figure 42 in Appendix A.7. This out-
put consists of waste streams that are generated from the unit process, including disposal,
recycling, and combustion, etc.

==PAGE:42==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
4.4.4
Sensitivity Analysis
It is essential to conduct a sensitivity analysis during the LCI phase to evaluate the accuracy
of inputs and outputs based on certain assumptions. Especially when different assumptions
were made in the data collection. The goal is to identify which parameters need precise
knowledge for valid conclusions.
Here a One-at-A-Time (OAT) approach is used, which
change one input parameter at a time to determine how significantly that parameter influ-
ences the final result. This local sensitivity analysis estimates output changes based on the
specific LCA model [77].
4.4.5
Additional Information for the Optimized Model
The differences in some aspects of the new design are described in subsubsection 4.1.2.
This also affects the data collection for the LCA. The following sections will describe the
changed processes, while the others will remain as previously described. Calculations for
the adjusted processes are detailed in Appendix A.4. Due to limited information, it should
be noted that only the optimization of the main station box will be addressed (see Figure 12).
Secant Piles
As mentioned in subsubsection 4.1.2, the diameter of the secant piles has decreased from
1.18 m to 1.00 m, requiring additional piles since the excavation pit area remains unchanged.
From Table 9, it is known that 151 secondary secant piles were produced on the site. How-
ever, only 101 of these piles were located in the main station box (based on their volume from
Kronos and Figure 12). Consequently, the original volume of the secant piles are presented
in Table 9, equal to 4.41·103 m3. 3.04·103 m3 represent the volume of what is left in the main
box, indicating that 557 m3 of secondary piles are outside the main box and will remain the
same, since no sufficient information regarding the shallow box is given. For the primary
piles 1.37·103 m3 is outside the box. For detailed calculation see Table 46 in Appendix A.4.
The total circumference of the construction pit is 182 m according to the reference model
(based on the pile information), and the secondary pile overlap with 0.62 m from the pri-
mary pile (S - D from Figure 8). Based on this information, an increase of 12 secant piles
is necessary for the optimized design to maintain the same circumference when the updated
diameter of the secondary pile is equal to 1 meter. As a result, the total volume of sec-
ondary piles has decreased from 3.62·103 m3 to 2.81·103 m3, while the volume of primary
piles has increased from 4.41·103 m3 to 4.59·103 m3 (see details in Table 46 in Appendix A.4).
As the amount of secondary piles has changed the reinforcement also needs to change. The
calculations for the reinforcement is described in subsection 4.2. However, due to the data
collection for the LCA it is also needed to be mentioned here. The reinforcement calculation
was only performed for the section in focus, DS3A (refer to Figure 12). The remaining
reinforcement has been changed proportional to this reinforcement due to time and limited
information. Regarding the reinforcement for the piles in DS3A reinforcement type 2 is
needed [42]. This reinforcement contains of main and temporary reinforcement, the tempo-
rary reinforcement will remain the same and is equal to 136 kg. The main reinforcement type
2 has decreased from 6.51·103 kg [60] to 4.68·103 kg for the new design (refer to Table 47 in
Appendix A.4 for calculations). This results in a savings of 28% in reinforcement per pile.

are positioned in DS3A and DS3B, as this cross-section dominates the main box, as shown

Anchors
Since the second anchor level has been removed and additional piles are required in the new
design, the process for the anchors also needs to be reconsidered. From above it is known
that the primary piles in DS3A and DS3B are increased with 6 piles. This leads to an in-
crease of 6 anchors at the anchor level. The weight of the wires has therefore decreased from
one tonne to 581 kg with the new design, shown in Table 49 in Appendix A.4.
Additionally, the volume of grout has reduced from 26.5 m3 to 15.4 m3 (detailed calculation
shown in Table 50 in Appendix A.4). The weight of the steel plate has also decreased from
a total of 1.11 · 104 kg to 6.12 · 103 kg (see Table 48 in Appendix A.4). Furthermore, the
working hours recorded in the database have been reduced by 45% due to a 45% decrease in
the number of anchors.
Strut
Regarding the strut, only the third level in DS3A (the perpendicular struts) has changed.
The remaining struts is the same. The strut has increased from a spacing equal to 6.50 m to
10.0 m. As the DS3A cross-section is 48.6 m long, the number of struts has decreased from
eight to five. Table 51 in Appendix A.4 shows the new required amount of struts by weight.

4.5
Impact Assessment of Environmental Assessment
With all the inputs and outputs described in subsection 4.4, the Life Cycle Impact Assess-
ment can begin. For this study, the SimaPro software were used where the methods utilized
are ”ReCiPe 2016 Midpoint (H) V1.09 / World (2010) H” and ”ReCiPe 2016 Endpoint (H)
V1.09/ World (2010) H/A”. Both approaches adopt a hierarchist perspective (H) based on
widely accepted policy principles concerning the time frame.
The ReCiPe 2016 World (H/A) method was chosen for its comprehensive coverage of impact
categories and its incorporation of weighting to produce a single score result. For a detailed
description of the impact categories, please refer to the SimaPro Methods library [29]
4.6
Social Impacts of the Design
The second sustainable pillar to consider is the social pillar, which involves examining how
changes in design will affect workers’ processes and working conditions. The time aspect is
also crucial, as social benefits from the final product will reach users sooner with a design
that can be implemented more quickly.
Additionally, noise is an important factor when considering social aspects. In the Kronos
database, noise reports were collected for about seven months in 2019, and this data is
presented in Appendix A.2.
4.7
Budget Analysis of Economic Assessment
The economic aspect for this project is based on a budget, with the primary reference for
the budget drawn from Spon’s Price Book [78]. The book reflects the Great Britain (GB)
rate and was published in 2018. This edition is deemed appropriate, as material costs ref-
erence sources dating to early 2019 (see the Notification of Material of the concrete [58]).
Furthermore, the focus of the study is specifically on comparing the optimized design with
the reference design.
Spon’s Price Book provides cost estimates for the construction industry, including machine
operation for various processes, thereby simplifying budget creation. However, this study
excludes overhead costs such as headquarters, accounting, management, and labor hours,
concentrating instead on materials and the establishment of processes.
Since the budget is derived from the GB rate obtained from Spon’s Price Book, it is converted
to Danish krone (DKK) at an exchange rate of 1 GBP = 8.95 DKK [79], as of November
29, 2024. It is notable that the costs referenced are from 2018, during which the exchange
rate was 1 GBP = 8.30 DKK [80]. Thus, the exchange rate sourced from 2024 is considered
valid, especially in light of the price increases by post-COVID-19.
38/ 83
==PAGE:45==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
For the secant piles, Spon’s Price Book only considers diaphragm walls as a solution. This
has been used as the best fit. The costs utilized here are based on an excavated volume of
4.00·103 m3 using standard equipment. Excavation, soil disposal, and concrete placement,
along with the provision of reinforcement cages, are included in the calculation.
Regarding the anchors, the costs are based on the installation of 50 ground anchors, with a
maximum depth of 15 meters, along with the establishment of standard equipment and the
associated removal costs.
For the excavated soil, a disposal volume of over 10.0·103 m is considered, excavation and
back filling, as well as disposal and transport costs, are included in the calculations.
The study considers trench struts under temporary works, with costs based on the rental of
modular systems, including an allowance for consumable materials [78].
Due to the unavailability of information about waler beams, a price from St˚alXperten is
employed [81]. This reference is solely for materials, which is acceptable, as the optimization
of the this process in this study will not change with the new design.
It has not been possible to ascertain the properties of the base slab. Therefore, Unicon’s
price list for 2023 [82] has been utilized, albeit only for material costs. The price for the
C40/50 Standard Products is applied, as this type has been used for the base slab at Havne-
holmen station [42].

5.1.2
Reference Model: Soil Stability
When constructing a deep excavation, it is crucial to consider how the soil reacts to the
imposed loads from the structure. Figure 20 illustrates the plastic points for the last con-
struction stage, while the remaining stages are presented in Appendix C.1.1. The plastic
points indicate the transition of the material from elastic to plastic behavior, with the red
points signifying the failure points and the white points denoting the tension cut-off points.
In subsection 4.1, it was stated that the Plaxis 2D model utilized the Mohr-Coulomb ap-
proach.
Consequently, the failure points align with the failure envelope defined by the
Mohr-Coulomb criterion.
The tension cut-off points indicate locations where the structure or soil can no longer with-
stand tensile stresses. This situation arises when the materials are overloaded in tension,
resulting in plastic deformation that exceeds the permissible strain limit for the material.
Identifying these points is essential for assessing potential failure within the excavation.
Figure 20: Overview over plastic points for the last construction stage for the reference
model.
The red points illustrate the failure points, while the white points indicate the
tension cut-off points: both are classified as plastic points.
To determine whether these plastic points have actually reached the plastic zone, the in-
cremental deviatoric strain in Plaxis 2D can be utilized, as this plot illustrates the plastic
deformation that has occurred. Figure 21 shows the final construction stage for the refer-
ence model, including the incremental deviatoric strain, the remaining stages can be found
in Appendix C.1.2. The maximum value in Figure 21 is 0.66·10−4 while the minimum value
is 2.24 · 10−9. The maximum value for all the stages in Appendix C.1.2 is 4.49·10−6, while
the minimum value is 7.11·10−9.
44/ 83
==PAGE:51==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Figure 21: Overview over incremental deviatoric strains for the last construction stage for
the reference model.

Results of the Environmental Assessment
The results from the reference model from the LCA will be outlined in the subsequent section

5.2.1
Reference Model LCA Results
To identify areas for optimization in the design phase of Havneholmen, it is necessary to

5.3
Social Impact Findings
With the optimal design, the construction sequence will decrease from nine main steps (sec-
tion 3) to seven main steps (subsubsection 4.1.2). Additionally, it has been assessed that
12 more secant piles are needed in the optimized model, these two changes impact the con-
struction timeline.
In practice, the process of boring the piles is often the most time-consuming aspect com-
pared to filling the bored holes with concrete and reinforcement if necessary. Since the new
design includes 12 additional piles, this phase will take approximately 12 days longer, as it is
estimated that one pile takes around one day to construct. This additional time may be due
to factors such as the preparation of the drilling equipment and the drilling process itself,
which might take longer because of the limestone. However, reducing the number of steps
in the construction sequence will help optimize time in other aspects.
It has also been observed that the strut spacing has increased from 6.50 meters to 10.0
meters, which will affect the time, working process, and the number of needed struts. The
influence of these elements will be discussed further in subsection 6.3.
Appendix A.1 illustrates the results of the noise reports, indicating that the noise limit was
exceeded daily from six in the morning until late at night over the seven months of noise
monitoring.
