6
Discussion
In the following sections, the validation of the Plaxis 2D model will be discussed in relation
to the monitoring data and the original design.
The interpenetration phase of the LCA will include the hotspot analysis. The results will be
validated based on comparisons with existing studies, and mid- and endpoint comparisons
will be made between the reference and optimized models. Lastly, a sensitivity analysis will
highlight the most sensitive processes.
Consequently, the social and economic aspects will be discussed, with an emphasis on ro-
bustness. Finally, an overall performance assessment will be outlined.
6.1
Sustainable Solutions in Geotechnical Analysis
It is important to note that the results obtained from this study are expected to differ from
those outlined in the Structural Analysis Report [42], with relevant results shown in Ap-
pendix F taken directly from the Structural Analysis Report. The discrepancy arises among
others from the different software programs used. The models in this study are conducted
in Plaxis 2D, while the original design utilizes a pseudo-FEM software called FREW [42]. A
significant difference between the two software packages is that FREW incorporates a back-
calculation method to predict soil stiffness, whereas Plaxis 2D employs the Mohr-Coulomb
model does not [83]. Consequently, when following ULS LC2 and SLS, which both utilizes
lower bound values for soil material, the reference model applies these values throughout the
entire calculation.
One layer worth examining in relation to the underestimation of stiffness is the fill layer.
In the GIR report [41], the Young’s Modulus for the fill layer is set at 8.00 MPa for the
characteristic value, also shown in Table 5, which is considered low given that buildings have
previously been constructed at the area (outlined in subsection 3.3). Additionally, the GIR
report indicates that the layer has limited soil testing. This specify that the upper soil in
the area might have higher stiffness characteristics.
In subsubsection 4.1.1 is it stated that most of the soil materials utilize tension cut-off in
the Plaxis 2D, which indicates that the material cannot withstand certain tensile stresses
leading to soil deformation. This means that when using the feature in Plaxis 2D, the soil
will have a greater tendency to deform compared to a material that can withstand tensile
forces, resulting in increased deformation.
Due to the lack of information regarding the
model from the Structural Analysis Report, this tension cut-off may be one reason for the
difference in the results.
6.1.1
Displacement
The maximum displacement from the soil model is found at the top of the wall in the last
stage, this value is equal to 52.2 mm for the ULS LC2 case (see Figure 17b). Where the SLS
in the Structural Analysis Report is found to be around 29.0 mm, see Figure 79 in Appendix
F[42], this value is close to the maximum displacement found for the reference SLS case in
Figure 18b equal to 30.8 mm. This overall leads to conformity with the results from the
Structural Analysis Report.
56/ 83
==PAGE:63==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Considering the alert and alarm levels for horizontal displacement on the retaining walls
outlined in Table 25 from the Monitoring Plan [44], it is seen that most of the obtained
horizontal displacement from the reference model does not exceed the calculated displace-
ment along with the alarm and alert values. This holds true for excavations down to -3.50
mDVR90 and -10.8 mDVR90. The phase to excavate down to -16.5 mDVR90 exceed the
alarm value for the ULS LC2. Regarding the last excavation stage in Table 25 the calculated
displacement for the ULS LC2 is under the alarm value but higher than the alert value,
where the SLS do not exceeds any of the alert/alarm values for all the stages.
This shows that the applied safety factors to the loads may be over-dimensioned, resulting
in an excessively conservative design due to exceeding the alarm value for the ULS LC2 case.
This load overestimation appears to be valid, as the dead load specified in EN1991 is given
as equal to 5 kN/m2 [84] and not 20 kN/m2 which is case for the SLS calculation. One way
to avoid this overestimation could be to align SLS values with the alert values while ensuring
ULS LC2 values reach the alarm values.

Going from comparing one point with another at a certain stage, to consider the whole
wall displacement leads to another behavior. Figure 27 shows the deformations obtained
when the bottom of the excavation has been reached. The red line in the figure represent
the SLS case, where the blue line is for the ULS LC2 case, both for the reference model.
The dashed yellow line shows the observed measurements from the inclinometer at the same
stage, which is located nearest the analyzed cross-section. The fully retracted yellow line
is the SLS calculations obtained from the FREW. The black and dotted grey line is not
included in the analysis. It is observed that the deflections of the wall for the SLS case are
shifted by 5.00 mm from the inclinometer measurements along the wall, and at the top, the
displacement has increased by 50%. This indicates that the SLS calculations from Plaxis 2D
yield conservative results, suggesting that a more flexible structure is available.
Another factor that might affect the horizontal displacements, other than the load and stiff-
ness of the soil in the reference model could be the modeling of anchors. In the models, the
anchors are implemented using a Node-to-node anchor system, followed by an Embedded
beam row, as depicted in Figure 13. However, the results indicates that the anchor exhibits
movement, which would not be observed in practice, especially considering the large fixed
anchor length in the limestone.

The same comparison has been made for the optimized model, where the results are provided
in Appendix G.2. It is observed that the same behavior regarding horizontal displacement
occurs. However, when considering the displacement at one point for the optimized ULS
LC2 case (see Table 54 in Appendix G.2), none of the measured displacements reach any
of the alert/alarm values, taking into account that these levels are proportional to the ex-
cavation level. This suggests that the alert/alarm values could potentially be lowered when
considering the optimized design. Additionally, it is noted that the displacement through
the stages of the anchors (also shown in Appendix G.2) is 1.07 mm lower compared to the
reference model, resulting in a stronger model.
Figure 83 in Appendix G.2 shows the deformation along the wall in relation to the opti-
mized model. In the new design, the SLS case deflects less than the measurements from
the inclinometers, again indicating that a more flexible structure could theoretically occur.
However, even though a more flexible structure is possible concerning the wall deflection,
the structural design has reached its limits. Decreasing the thickness of the wall will not
meet the cage requirements for the secant piles in accordance with Eurocode.
6.1.2
Anchor and Strut Forces
The forces acting on the anchors and struts obtained from the Plaxis 2D model are pre-
sented in Table 26. It is evident that the anchor forces consistently increase throughout the
construction phases. In contrast, the strut forces experience a sharp rise in the final phase,
indicating that the struts are carrying a lot of the load at that point.

of alert/alarm levels and calculated values that lays between the alert and alarm values. The
values in Table 26 are lower than those in the Structural Analysis Report, as the values in
Table 26 do not include any safety factors. However, according to Eurocode 7 [7], the design
value for the anchor force can be found below.
Fp = ξ(γaKFI)αFad,res
(1)
In the ULS LC2 cases, ξ is equal to 1.10 and γa is equal to 1.30, in accordance with the inves-
tigation tests required for ground anchors [85]. The consequence factor, KFI, varies between
1.00 and 1.10, depends on the consequence class. α is equal to one and Fad,res is the anchor

force from Table 26 multiplied by the center-to-center distance and the angle of the anchor.
Using Equation 1, it is noted that the anchor forces now range between 200-350 kN under
the obtained values from the Structural Analysis Report, which is considered acceptable (see
Table 56 in Appendix G.2 for calculations). The strut force is also considered acceptable, as
this value is slightly smaller than the one obtained from the Structural Analysis Report.
The optimal forces are shown in Table 55 in Appendix G.2, where an increase in the force
of approximately 30.0 kN in relation to those obtained in Table 26, suggests that the same
alarm/alert values can be used with the new design. All of this indicates that the overall
results from both the reference and optimized models in Plaxis 2D, in relation to the anchor
and strut forces, are sufficient.
6.1.3
Bending Moment
The reference bending moment envelope obtained in this study, illustrated in Figure 19a,
shows that the positive bending moment is approximately half the magnitude of the value
derived using the FREW software, as depicted in Figure 82 in Appendix F. In contrast, the
absolute difference between the maximum and minimum values obtained from Figure 19a
reaches 4.76·103 kNm. In comparison, the Structural Analysis Report only provides the value
from Figure 82, resulting in some uncertainty when interpreting the findings. Nonetheless,
an absolute value of approximately 5.20·103 kNm is determined from the Structural Analy-
sis Report, leading to a difference of 500 kNm. This discrepancy is considered acceptable,
particularly in light of the uncertainties previously highlighted.
The two models with Fixed-end anchors (see Appendix I) do not show a greater absolute
difference in the bending moment envelope compared to the reference model, as expected
due to the previously noted reduced displacement. In contrast, the model with horizontal
anchors is the only Plaxis 2D model that exhibits the highest absolute moment difference
between the two anchor levels, a result that is also obtained from the FREW calculations.
Overall, it is assessed that the Plaxis 2D models provide suitable results for the design used
for the Havneholmen station, considering the aims of the models in this study. However, the
modeling of different anchor approaches suggests that the Plaxis 2D model may not be as
robust as expected, which contributes to the discrepancies between the monitored data and
the models. These observed differences emphasize the need for careful selection of modeling
parameters to ensure reliability in design outcomes.
6.1.4
Reinforcement Design
Given that the secondary piles require reinforcement, a detailed analysis of their design is
essential. The design of reinforcement cages has been addressed, where the reference model
utilized two column sections for flexural reinforcement and four column sections for shear
reinforcement, shown in subsubsection 5.1.1. In contrast, the optimized model (see subsub-
section 5.1.3) increased to three column sections for flexural reinforcement while maintaining
the same number of sections for shear reinforcement. While an increase in the number of
column sections may reduce the amount of steel used in the piles, thereby lowering environ-
mental impacts, this also introduces practical challenges on the construction site.
60/ 83
==PAGE:67==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
The installation of various reinforcement types within a bored hole that is approximately
26.0 meters deep poses significant challenges, requiring different safety factors, including
substantial concrete cover. Moreover, as the variety of reinforcement types increases per
pile, the process becomes slower, ultimately leading to higher costs that negatively affect
economic feasibility.
6.1.5
Investigating Plasticity in Soil
The plastic points were described in subsubsection 5.1.2, and the reference model’s plastic
points for its final phase are shown in Figure 20, while the optimized model is presented in
Figure 25. It is evident that both models exhibit failure points under the surface load arising
from Fisketorvet. This is expected, as this substantial load is sufficient to induce plastic soil
behavior.
It is further observed from the figures that the first level of anchors beneath Fisketorvet has
failure points along its fixed length, likely due to the large surface load affecting the anchors.
Another reason for the plastic points in the fixed length may be attributed to the observed
displacements in that region, as described in subsubsection 6.1.1, which were assessed to be
due to numerical issues rather than real-world conditions. This observation is expected since
the anchors are designed to transfer load to the soil to stabilize the wall.
In contrast, the reference model shows that the second anchor level does not exhibit any
plastic points in the fixed length. This may be related to the soil parameters, as the second
anchor level is located solely in the Upper Copenhagen Limestone, while the first level also
extends into the Glacially Disturbed Limestone. The latter has a lower tensile strength com-
pared to the Upper Copenhagen Limestone, likely making it less capable of withstanding the
applied loads from the first anchor. Consequently, the softer Glacially Disturbed Limestone
may reach its plastic limit before the stronger Upper Copenhagen Limestone, which could
explain why the first level of anchors exhibits plastic points in the fixed length, while the
second anchor level does not.
On the other side of the excavation, fewer failure points are observed, due to less deformation
from the surface load. However, this area shows a greater number of tension cut-off points
in the anchors. This may be due to the anchors transmitting significant tensile stress to the
soil while stabilizing the wall, causing the soil to reach its tensile stress limit and become
unable to withstand these forces any longer.
Both sides of the excavation shows a potential failure surface line which is not fully devel-
oped, and this line also goes over the free length of the anchors, suggesting that the structure
remains stable. It is also noticed that the poteintail faiilure line starts at the excavation sur-
face, resulting in the failure line is not critical.
Furthermore, it is noted that the optimized design in Figure 25 has fewer plastic points on
the side without Fisketorvet surface load, while the side with Fisketorvet has nearly the
same number of plastic points as the reference model. Overall, it can be assessed that the
structure exhibits a similar vulnerability to failure in both cases.
61/ 83
==PAGE:68==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
The plastic points indicate where the possibility of the material entering plastic behavior
may occur, while the incremental deviatoric strain shows where actual deformation takes
place. Figure 21 presents the final stage of the reference model for incremental deviatoric
strain, while the optimized design is shown in Figure 26. It is observed that both designs
fall within a range that is essentially zero, indicating that no significant change in shape will
occur and that the soil will remain in elastic behavior, thus validating both designs.
6.1.6
Recommendations and Limitations
It is mentioned that the difference in software used in this study and that used when de-
signing the station might be one reason for some discrepancies. It could be relevant to gain
more insight into the model programmed in FREW, as it seems that the model had some
boundary conditions, such as the shear force being zero at the bottom of the wall. Further-
more, how the anchors were modeled would also be relevant to investigate how conservative
this model was designed.
The optimized design is only considered until the base slab is constructed. However, it would
be relevant to investigate whether the optimized design could withstand the remaining de-
sign process, such as removing the temporary strut system, with the aim of determining if
the design could be used in practice or if it is only possible in theory.
It is also noted that the concrete cover has been changed from 90.0 mm to 40.0 mm to reduce
the thickness of the piles. It could be interesting to investigate why Metroselskabet initially
adopted the conservative approach of 90.0 mm and what is needed to decrease it to 40.0
mm in accordance with the standards of Eurocode 2. In the same context, it would also be
interesting to look into what was required to incorporate the secant piles into the permanent
structures so that their use could be fully utilized. These considerations might require some
experimentation as well, but they could hopefully lead to a better solution in relation to the
environment and enhance sustainability insights.
When examining soil stability, it is observed that the response of the soil is acceptable,
however, no safety analysis has been performed in this study. This seems to be acceptable
given the current analysis. However, for further studies, it may be essential to conduct a
safety analysis.
6.2
Interpretation of the Environmental Impacts
With the LCA results presented in subsubsection 5.2.1, the environmental interpretation of
the assessment for Havneholmen can now begin.
6.2.1
Identification of Hotspots
With the characterization values for the reference model obtained from the midpoint (Ta-
ble 19) and endpoint (Table 20) analyses, it is observed that the secant piles had the highest
values in all relevant impact categories. The results indicate that the secant piles contribute
the most to these categories, suggesting that this process is the hotspot for this analysis and
should be prioritized in the optimization model.
62/ 83
==PAGE:69==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
After the secant piles, the base slab contributes the most followed by the struts and beams,
as observed in the results section, subsection 4.5. However, the data for the base slab is
limited, since the only information available is the concrete type and the dimensions, but no
information regarding the reinforcement is given.
The processes that contribute the least are the connections (which consists of bolts and
welding) and waste, which will therefore not be examined further in relation to optimization.
To minimize impacts across multiple categories, optimizing both concrete and steel in the
secant piles is recommended. This strategy aligns with the project’s overall environmental
goals.
6.2.2
Comparison with Existing Studies
Results from other studies are needed to ensure the validation of the results obtained from
the LCA analysis in this study. Here are four different studies related to civil engineering,
with the fourth serving as a baseline for comparison. These studies are used to assess the
findings of the reference model and determine the validity of the results.
Norwegian Tunnel
In the first study, Huang (2015) [86] assessed impacts such as global warming, ozone de-
pletion, human toxicity, and terrestrial acidification using the ReCiPe 2008 Midpoint (E)
methodology and the EcoInvent database for background data. The study focused on the
life cycle effects of a standard road tunnel in Norway, with the functional unit defined as ”1
meter of standard Norwegian road tunnel with a 100-year lifespan.” The values in Table 27
represent some of the inputs and outputs derived from Huang (2015) for a Norwegian stan-
dard road tunnel, characterized by a cross-sectional area of 67.0 m2 and a length of three
kilometers. The table also shows the inputs and outputs for Havneholmen. The results in
the table are given pr cubic meter to be able to compare the results with this study later
on, which would be the same pr. FU for Havneholmen.

The choice of the Egalitarian (E) method in Huang (2015), rather than the Hierarchist (H)
approach used in this study, introduces some differences. The Egalitarian perspective is
generally more precautionary regarding future socio-economic developments over a span of
63/ 83
==PAGE:70==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
500 years, while the Hierarchist serves as a baseline, focusing on short-term economic ben-
efits for a period of 100 years [87]. Other than that, the ReCiPe 2008 method employs a
European scale for characterization factors, whereas ReCiPe 2016 utilizes a global scale, and
the reference years differ with 10 years [29]. This variation leads to some differences in some
of the categorizations, among others the human toxicity, which is divided into carcinogenic
and non-carcinogenic in the 2016 edition, while the old edition merges these two categories
[28]. Huang (2015) also spans a range between ’cradle-to-site’ and ’cradle-to-grave’ (shown in
Figure 2), whereas this study focuses solely on a ’cradle-to-site’ perspective. Consequently,
Huang’s analysis accounts for maintenance and energy use in the operation phase, again
leading to higher expected outputs compared to this study, the results used for the following
comparison is shown in Table 27.
Variation in results between the two studies are therefore expected due to methodological
differences. The inputs in the two studies in Table 27 varies a lot, which might be due to
the different construction types. Havneholmen uses more steel per cubic meter as an input.
This is likely due to the presence of steel in the anchors, cages in the secant piles and the
struts and beams used for constructing the Havneholmen. In contrast, for the Norwegian
tunnel, steel is primarily used for rock support and tunnel lining. The increased concrete
used in Havneholmen may be attributed to the base slab, which is absent in road tunneling
projects. Additionally, Table 27 indicates that the tunnel project has a higher grout volume
(total of 1.20·106 kg), as grout is essential for waterproofing and stabilizing the tunnel [86].
Where for Havneholmen only an amount of 6.02·104 kg is used in total.
The varying ratios of inputs in Table 27 demonstrate that most outputs maintain a consistent
ratio, however, Human toxicity is significantly higher for the Norwegian tunnel. Initially, it
may seem that grout contributes the most to this toxicity. However, when considering only
the anchor results in this study (see Figure 52 in Appendix B.6.2), it becomes clear that
grout is not the dominant sub-process affecting the Human toxicity impact category.
Additionally, as illustrated in Figure 5 of Huang (2015), Human toxicity is primarily driven
by electricity usage for ventilation and lighting during the operational stage, with grout con-
tributing only minimally. The system boundary for this study does not include electricity.
The secant piles are the only process that contains both steel and concrete. Figure 28 illus-
trates how the sub-processes from one secant pile affect the different impact categories. The
remaining impact categories for one pile are shown in Figure 51 in Appendix B.6.1. With
the figure it is observed that the environmental impact categories are primarily dominated
by steel and concrete, with concrete contributing approximately 60% of the global warming.
Figure 53 in Appendix B.6.3 illustrates the distribution of all the sub-process sums combined,
still demonstrates that steel and concrete contribute the most to all impact categories. This
also indicates that focusing on just one secant pile is sufficient.
According to Figure 5 in Huang (2015), concrete accounts for around 40% of global warming,
while electricity contributes approximately with 20%. Figure 28 shows that steel significantly
contributes to human toxicity, which aligns with expectations, as steel production is known
to emit more harmful chemicals related to human toxicity [88]. However, Huang (2015)
shows that the steel only represent with 15% in human toxicity, while electricity dominating
with 60%. Furthermore, Huang (2015) states that the operation phase, particularly the elec-
tricity for ventilation and lighting, negatively affects environmental impacts, emphasizing
64/ 83
==PAGE:71==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
the relevance of looking at the operational phase for the metro stations and metro lines in
achieving the 50% reduction in CO2 emissions, which Metroselskabet wants.
Figure 28: Distribution of most relevant impact categories obtained from one secant pile.
Overall, the values obtained in this study remain acceptable in comparison with Huang
(2015), considering uncertainties and the lack of specific material details in Huang (2015).
These observations underscore the significance of system boundaries and highlight the rele-
vance of being specific in material choices. The importance of material specification will be
discussed in the Sensitivity Analysis later on.

in the piles, and the soil displaced (31.9 m3) during the pile installation. The outputs for a
drilled shaft in clay for global warming and terrestrial acidification from Misra (2010) and
Havneholmen are presented in Table 28. The values for Havneholmen are presented in the

to ensure that future studies can effectively use the data. It is unclear if Liu (2020) includes

High-Rise Building
To obtain the best validation of the results from this study, benchmark results would have
been ideal rather than solely comparing some of the findings with other studies. However,
as Liu (2020) [24] describes, there is an absence of such quantification due to the complexity
of the LCA, which arises from the numerous materials and processes involved, making it
difficult to establish benchmark results. While process-based LCAs ideally provide greater
accuracy, they are challenging to complete fully since comprehensive inventory data is typi-
cally unavailable at the planning stage [24].
Since benchmark results cannot be obtained, a baseline system from Tainya (2020) [90]
is considered. Tainya (2020) focuses on a high-rise building in Tehran, Iran. The system
boundaries were established from the transportation of building materials from the factory to
the construction site and throughout the construction phase. The functional unit is defined
as ”1 m2 of Gross Floor Area”, where the Gross Floor Area is equal 3.00·104 m2. Tainya
(2020) uses the ReCiPe 2008 method but nothing is stated regarding the used database. The
relevant inputs are shown in Table 30 along with the output from the midpoint method, the
remaning outputs can be found directly in Tainya (2020).

6.2.3
Environmental Benefit of the Optimized Model, Midpoint
With the obtained midpoint characterization values for the reference model (Table 19) and
the optimized model (Table 21), the overall savings across the different impact categories can
be calculated and are presented in Figure 29. The fully shaded values indicate the reference
model, where the desaturated values are for the optimized model. The remaining results
for the midpoint impact categories are presented in Figure 46 in Appendix B.2, while the
results for the optimized model are shown in Figure 48 in Appendix B.4.
In Figure 29, it can be seen that Human carcinogenic toxicity has the highest savings, at
13.0%, whereas Land use shows the least savings, with the optimized model saving only
7.10%. While Metroselskabet has a vision of reducing overall CO2 emissions by 50%, this
may suggest that optimizing the design of the deep excavation, will not have a major impact,
especially when the optimized design only saved 8.00% for global warming. Conversely, ex-
amining electricity usage in the operation phase might be a crucial element for the reduction
of CO2 emissions, based on the comparison with Huang (2015).
Figure 29: Distribution of the relevant impact categories for the midpoint results. Results
from the reference model are equal to 100%, while the optimized model results are presented
in relation to the reference model value, marked with desaturated colors.
When considering the midpoint characterization values in the figure above, it is observed
that the secant piles remain the dominant contributors, even though the design has been
optimized. This is expected, as the process still requires the most concrete and steel, and the
secant piles also represent the largest volume. It is clear that the optimized design does not
significantly change the distribution of the different processes across the impact categories.
However, the environmental pillar demonstrates a lower impact, with the optimize model,
which is worth examining. It is still worth examining what has caused the changes in the
impact categories with the optimized design, which will be addressed in the following sections.
When looking into the midpoint characterization values for Land use in Figure 29, it is evi-
dent that the secant piles and the base slab, significantly contribute to the depletion of land
resources, which is the processes that involves the production of steel and concrete. This
indicates that a substantial area of land is generally required to produce these elements, this
69/ 83
==PAGE:76==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
could among others be due to the sand pits, which is an essential components for concrete
and available in Denmark. However, it is known that Denmark is running low on these
resources.
The Land use category reinforces that a considerable amount of land is required to produce
such materials. This suggests that the sand (and gravel) resources are depleting more rapidly
in Denmark, along with an increase in the construction industry, leading to a greater reliance
on importing these construction materials from abroad.
As the scarcity of land resources arises in other parts of the world, this can result in higher
prices and potential supply chain disruptions. Therefore, it underscores the importance of
sustainable measures for processes that consume significant amounts of concrete. Strategies
may include improving material recycling, developing alternative building materials, and
optimizing designs and construction techniques to reduce reliance on these critical resources.
It is important to note that while sand and gravel are not inherently limited in Denmark,
the real issue lies in defining where these materials can be excavated. Consequently, national
resource plans are necessary to address these challenges[91].
If these resources in the future is locally missed, they must be imported from other countries,
which increase the length of transportation significantly, further escalating the consumption
of fossil resources. This indicates that finding a sustainable solution is necessary, which
also include converting machine operation, transport, and manufacturing etc. from diesel to
electric devices would be beneficial.
Figure 46 in Appendix B.2 includes the other categories like Freshwater Eutrophication, Ma-
rine Eutrophication, and Terrestrial Acidification, where it is observed that these categories
exhibit similar distributions of processes as Land use. This observation is not surprising, as
land use significantly impacts these categories. Changes in land use can affect the health
of ecosystems and their natural processes, potentially leading to increased nutrient levels in
waterways and changes in soil composition that influence eutrophication and acidification
[92].
The excavation process shows some relevance to the Fossil resource category, along with the
base slab, secant piles, struts, and beams observed in Figure 29. The excavation process
along with the secant piles, struts, and beams, as well as the anchors, involves machine
operation time and transportation, which is powered by diesel in this assessment, contribut-
ing to fossil resource consumption. Indicating that this is the reason why these processes
dominates the Fossil resource category.
However, this is not the case for the secant piles. Figure 28 shows that the secant pile process
is dominated by the reinforcement steel and concrete production. These two sub-processes
also incorporates the overall manufacturing of these sub-process which uses a lot of fossil
resources due to the substantial machine and process operation involved in their production.
Resulting in this observation is valid for the secant piles.
In Figure 29, it can be seen that the greatest savings is from the impact category Human
carcinogenic toxicity, followed by Mineral resources (11.7%). The reduction in steel used in
the anchors, struts, beams, and secant piles primarily accounts for this decrease.
Producing steel requires ore, which necessitates significant amounts of these mineral re-
sources. This ore is extracted from mines, where the mining process itself also has high
environmental consequences due to the considerable energy and materials involved in ore
mining. Some of these processes emit dust and other pollutants that can adversely affect
70/ 83
==PAGE:77==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
human health, as some particles may be harmful to inhale, potentially leading to health
problems for workers in the industry or individuals living nearby [93]. This is one of the
reasons why steel dominates the metrics for human toxicity and mineral resources.
In Figure 46 in Appendix B.2, it is observed that the Human toxicity and ecotoxicity cat-
egories are quite similar, which is expected since many of the same pollutants can affect
different categories (see description in Appendix B.1).
However, it is important to note
that Terrestrial ecotoxicity is more influenced by the process struts and beams, as certain
toxins can accumulate in the land from the metal [94]. In contrast, the water pumps used
for groundwater control have a greater impact on Freshwater ecotoxicity since these pumps
might leak pollutants directly into freshwater sources.
The impact category of Ozone formation, is a critical factor in this type of LCA. The Tro-
pospheric ozone can have severe consequences for both human health and the environment.
Analyzing ozone formation enables the identification of how the selected materials contribute
to this form of air pollution, thereby informing decisions aimed at promoting more sustain-
able practices [95].
In this analysis, it is evident that concrete is the largest contributor to ozone formation,
demonstrated in Figure 28. This contribution is primarily due to nitrogen oxides (NOx)
emitted during concrete production. In 2010, Aalborg Portland, a cement factory in Den-
mark, discharged 2.00·106 kg of NOx pollutants, significantly exceeding the reference model’s
annual discharge of 226 kg NOx. Aalborg Portland stated that further reduction of NOx
emissions is difficult to achieve [96], highlighting the importance of considering alternative
building materials or methods to, minimize these emissions. Considering alternative building
materials that emits lower levels of ozone precursors can benefit future projects. Integrating
such materials, can not only reduce ozone formation but it also support broader sustainabil-
ity goals.
In Figure 46 in Appendix B.2, it is observed that the distribution of the different processes
in the categories of Ozone Formation, Terrestrial Ecosystems, and Stratospheric Ozone De-
pletion is similar to the category of Ozone Formation, Human Health. This similarity is
expected, as these impact categories are interconnected through the processes related to
ozone formation.
The concrete production itself also significantly contributes to the global warming (see Fig-
ure 28). This is not surprising, as the cement and concrete industry is known to be a major
source of environmental degradation due to the carbon dioxide released during cement man-
ufacturing [97]. To substantially reduce carbon dioxide emissions, alternative technologies
must be explored. There is ongoing research in this area, and Salim Barhuiya [97] highlights
some successful decarbonization initiatives. These initiatives face challenges, including tech-
nological hurdles and financing issues.
Figure 29 shows that water consumption has decreased by 8.80% with the optimized design,
while Figure 28 indicates that concrete contributes the most to the water consumption in
relation to the secant piles. This finding is not surprising, as the concrete industry generally
also uses a significant amount of water in the production.
However, it is difficult to be criticize about this observation since it is recognized that the
data from the water consumption are often limited concerning water inventory methodolo-
gies. The wide dispersion of inventory data for the concrete life cycle is partly due to the
71/ 83
==PAGE:78==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
many different methods used to measure water consumption during concrete production,
resulting in considerable variation in the reported data. This suggests that drawing con-
clusions regarding water consumption for this study is challenging. Nonetheless, it clearly
highlights the need for standardization in the near future. Such standardization would fa-
cilitate comparison and evaluation of different materials in the industry [98].
6.2.4
Environmental Benefit of the Optimized Model, Endpoint
It is known that the normalized endpoint values are smaller than the midpoint normalization
values. This results from the fact that the endpoint analysis focuses on a broader and more
holistic assessment of impacts on humans and ecosystems, whereas the midpoint analysis
concentrates more on specific environmental impacts. The endpoint results are derived from
the midpoint results and incorporate weighting factors that reflect the significance of differ-
ent types of damages and defined through a politically concerned [99].
With the obtained endpoint results presented in subsubsection 5.2.1 for both the reference
and optimized models, Figure 30 can be established.
This figure shows the normalized
endpoint results for the reference model, where the fully shaded values represent the reference
model and the desaturated values represent the optimized model. The figure indicates that
the distribution of dominating processes remains unchanged, which aligns with expectations
based on the midpoint results.
Figure 30: Distribution of the impact categories for the endpoint results. The reference
model is equal to 100%, while the optimized model results are presented in relation to the
reference model value, marked with desaturated colors.
72/ 83
==PAGE:79==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Human carcinogenic toxicity, Global warming, Human health, and Fine particulate matter
formation all significantly contribute to the endpoint single score, as shown in Figure 31 for
the reference model. The optimized model demonstrates similar behavior, which is presented
in Figure 49 in Appendix B.5. These impact categories are all connected to the damage cat-
egory Human health, suggesting that the weighting system is dominated by these factors,
leading to Human health being the expected dominant single score. This is clearly evident
in the reference model for the single score, as shown in Figure 47 in Appendix B.3, and for
the optimized model in Figure 50 in Appendix B.5. Indicating that the chosen weighting
system prioritizes Human health.
Figure 31: Contributions from impact categories towards the endpoint single score from the
reference model.
These varying results underscore the need for ongoing evaluation of material choices and de-
sign strategies to further mitigate environmental impacts in future projects. Understanding
the contributions of emissions, including ozone-depleting substances and CO2, is crucial for
making informed decisions that align with sustainability goals. Therefore, these observations
highlight the significance of exploring innovative design approaches and alternative materials
that can minimize the overall environmental footprint of construction activities.
Overall, it can be concluded that the optimized design achieves better environmental results
compared to the reference model, with the midpoint impact category of Human carcinogenic
toxicity showing the largest reduction of 13% for the optimized model.

==PAGE:80==

6.2.5
Influence of Steel in Base Slab
While identifying the hotspots for this study, it was observed that the base slab had some
uncertainties due to the limited information. The Structural Analysis Report [42] only pro-
vides the type of cement used, C40/50, and the dimensions. Therefore, this process needs to
undergo the One-at-A-Time approach (described in subsubsection 4.4.4), as it still has the
second largest impact on the final LCA (see subsubsection 5.2.1).
With the OAT approach all concrete types with the strengths between 40 and 50 MPa from
the EcoInvent database, along with the two processes labeled as ’concrete slab’ is considered.
This is done to identify those needing accurate or alternative validation, especially in light
of different data sources, assumptions, and unit processes that may introduce uncertainties.
Understanding these sensitivities is crucial for the robustness of the LCA, prompting a focus
on the implications of these validations for the overall analysis.
This results in seven different processes, as shown in Figure 32, with the results in the figure
being normalized. The remaining impact categories are shown in Figure 84 in Appendix H.
It is evident that the two ’concrete slab’ processes dominate, accounting for more than 50%
in all 18 impact categories. For instance, there is a significant difference of 700kg CO2eq
between the highest and lowest processes, highlighting the necessity of the sensitivity analysis
of this process.
Figure 32: Overview of normalization results from midpoint when considering all relevant
processes from the base slab with 1 kg.
74/ 83
==PAGE:81==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
When examining the details of what the concrete slab processes include, it becomes clear
that for 1 m3 concrete slab 0.98 m3 concrete is needed along with 80 kg reinforcing steel.
Indicating that the steel that is needed in reality is included. Both concrete and steel sig-
nificantly contribute to the various impact categories, meaning that the high values for the
concrete slab are both expected and acceptable. Since no information is provided about the
type of reinforcement used inside the base slab, the concrete slab processes are applied as the
best fit. Market values are utilized since no information is available regarding manufacturing
and transport.
Overall, the base slab’s environmental impact is significantly influenced by the materials
used, particularly steel. Choosing concrete alone may suggest a minor impact but could lead
to an inaccurate final assessment. This sensitivity analysis underscores the importance of
selecting appropriate materials for the base slab, as variations in steel quality can greatly
affect the findings. Identifying relevant processes is essential to understand how these ma-
terials influence overall environmental outcomes and ensure accurate results in the life cycle
assessment.
6.2.6
Choice of Steel Process
As the deep excavation consists of several different steel elements, various steel types from
the EcoInvent database are evaluated by the OAT. The normalized distribution is shown
in Figure 33, the rest of the impact categories are shown in Figure 85 in Appendix H. The
below figure highlights the necessity of a sensitivity analysis due to the choice of process
from the database, especially since some of the steel types contribute more significantly to
the impact categories compared to others. In the previous section, it was found how much
steel actually contributes to the environmental impact categories, and therefore, it needs to
be considered for all the processes using steel. The processes that include the most steel
will be discussed in the following, while the rest of the process evaluation can be found in
Appendix H.
75/ 83
==PAGE:82==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Figure 33: Normalized results for the midpoint, with all relevant steel processes included.
It is known that the hotspot lies in the secant piles, which include a certain amount of cages
made of steel, most likely from the processes referred to as ’reinforcing steel’. However,
choosing this process will have a higher environmental impact compared to selecting hot
rolling steel, shown in Figure 33, which therefore needs to be selected carefully. It is there-
fore necessary to examine the components of the reinforcing steel process. Each kilogram of
reinforcing steel requires 1.00 kg of hot rolling steel, 0.37 kg of low-alloyed steel, and 0.63
kg of unalloyed steel, resulting in a total input of 2.00 kg, while only 1.00 kg of reinforcing
steel is produced as output. This leads to 1.00 kg of waste, some of which may be reused.
Therefore, it is acceptable that the reinforcing steel processes result in higher environmental
emissions, as it contain several other steel processes.
In reviewing the two reinforcing steel types, one is sourced from the RoW (marked in orange
in the figure) and another from Europe without Austria (marked in dark blue in Figure 33).
The two processes shows similar values for the midpoint characterization values in Appendix
H (only a 5% difference). However, Ionizing radiation is the only category with significant
divergence, here the European reinforcing steel contributes with 41% more than the RoW
steel. This considerable difference may be due to the European process involving more ma-
terials that contribute to radiation.
Reinforcing steel, such as rebar and cages, is used for the anchor wires and cages in the piles.
It is assessed that the two steel types from the EcoInvent database have comparable influ-
ences and that using one instead of the other will not have a significant impact. However,
all manufacturers and suppliers considered for the study are located in Europe (Norway,
Germany, Sweden, and Spain) [65], leading to the conclusion that the European steel is the
best fit, despite its higher contribution to ionizing radiation.
76/ 83
==PAGE:83==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Regarding HEB and CHS beams, ’Steel, unalloyed GLO — market for steel, unalloyed’,
marked with green in Figure 33 is chosen for the LCA. It might have been more appropriate
to use ’Steel, unalloyed RoW — steel production, converter, unalloyed,’ (marked with light
blue in the figure) since 70% of the steel is produced using converter steel making [100]. The
reason for choosing the first process, which includes the ’market for steel’ process, is that
it encompasses both transport and manufacturing, for which no information is provided.
However it is observed that these two processes are more or less a like in Figure 33.
6.2.7
Recommendations and Limitations
Throughout the LCA, limitations have been highlighted, some of these are summarized here
along with recommendations. The various limitations identified during the LCA may have
simplified the model, potentially leading to some aspects being overlooked and limiting the
accuracy of the results.
In general, the selection of inputs in SimaPro related to machine operations involves some
uncertainties due to limited information regarding the number of machines utilized on-site.
According to Google Maps (see Figure 105 in Appendix J), it is confirmed that a large tower
crane was employed, which may significantly impact the overall machine operations. This
machine has not been included in the current assessment.
Additionally, The Weekly Monitoring Data from the Kronos database (see Table 8) reveals
that the piling work occurred from week 25 to 43 in 2019, taking 18 weeks (or 108 working
days) in total. Assuming an installation rate of one pile per day, only a third of the nec-
essary 338 secant piles would be completed, introducing uncertainties into the data. If the
Weekly Monitoring Data on installation times is correct (allowing for three drilling rigs at
the site), more machine operation should have been included in the environmental assess-
ment. Through the analysis, it is assessed that the machine operation does not significantly
affect the impacts. For further study, it would be more precise to include this factor or to
seek clarification from the company themselves.
The data collection for the analysis highlights how the HEB beams are most likely connected
to all the piles. However, since the anchors are placed at every primary pile, no beams are
needed to distribute the load at that position. This means that the HEB beams might only
be connected to all the piles on Level 3 of the reference design, while on Levels 1 and 2, only
HEB beams will be connected at the corners of the excavation pit. However, this information
does not seem to be clear in the available data, resulting in the HEB beams being connected
to all piles for all three levels, which may lead to some uncertainties, and in reality the struts
and beams would contribute less to the environmental impacts.
It is also known that the struts and beams are temporary and will be removed eventually.
Ideally, these struts and beams could be reused, however, it is assumed that these elements
will only be used once due to certain limitations. It would be interesting to assess the ex-
tent of damage that these elements sustain after use in Havneholmen to determine if they
could potentially be reused and if so, how many times. Additionally, investigating ways to
optimize their use before they are remelted would be worthwhile.
77/ 83
==PAGE:84==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Another significant environmental impact is the optimization of the base slab, which has
not been thoroughly addressed. The available data for this study only includes the concrete
type and dimensions of the base slab. However, it is known that a base slab requires rein-
forcement, which makes the available data quite uncertain. In this context, the sensitivity
analysis helped reduce the uncertainty. Additionally, the study observes that water pressure
has a substantial impact on the design of the excavation, as the water table is initially at the
level of -0.11 mDVR90 [41]. This raises concerns about the base slab, the initial thought was
to minimize its size using some form of updrift anchors due to the water, which could also
decrease the amount of needed concrete and steel. However, discussions with an employee
from Per Aarsleff, who helped constructing some of the metro lines in Sydhavnen revealed
that updrift anchors were already used for the base slab. If this is accurate, it suggests that
uncertainties and instances of misinformation might have arisen during this study.
Regardless of how the design for the base slab is structured, it would be relevant to inves-
tigate whether this type of process is designed in a standardized way. This could reveal
opportunities for easier optimization, especially since all metro stations needs a base slab
design.
Wang (2024) [94] investigated how mining soil has been contaminated by heavy metals. The
study showed that mining soil was contaminated up to twenty times higher than the back-
ground values due to heavy metal pollution. This indicates that the temporary structures
in Havneholmen, which will remain in the ground, contains a significant amount of steel
that should be examined. These materials will leach over time, potentially resulting in the
surrounding soil becoming polluted.
Wang (2024) also notes that with rainfall, the pollution from heavy metals can be washed
out into the water. This is particularly relevant to consider, as Havneholmen is located close
to the water in Copenhagen, where people swim during the summer, as well as affecting the
groundwater in general.
In relation to the LCIA method used, ReCiPe 2016, it is outlined that Human Health domi-
nates the single score. The data indicates that the endpoint normalization factor is 3.57·10−5
for the two resource categories, while the categories related to Human Health have an end-
point normalization factor of 41.7. This factor influences the final results shown in Figure 47
in Appendix B.2 and Figure 50 in Appendix B.4. Additionally, considering other weighting
factors, such as those used by Dong (2014) [99], can significantly alter the final results. This
underscores that weighting factors not only determine the contributions of damage categories
to the overall impact, but also influence the value of the single score. Furthermore, Dong
(2014) states that these weighting factors are defined by political concerns, indicating that
using alternative LCIA methods could reveal how much the final results actually vary.
6.3
Social Impact of Design Optimization on Construction
The optimized construction design has been reduced with two main steps in the construction
sequence, leading to a more streamlined process that benefits all stakeholders. Additionally,
the distance between the struts has increased by 3.50 meters, minimizing the risk of work-
related injuries and facilitates easier maneuverability for workers in the pit, improving safety
and working conditions. Transporting materials up and down from the bottom of the exca-
vation pit will also be more efficient, as the crane will have a wider workspace, leading to
78/ 83
==PAGE:85==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
optimized time utilization. As a result, it is likely that social acceptance of the project among
the workers will improve with the optimized design, which is crucial for the project’s success.
However, it must be emphasized that the social pillar for this study is not particularly robust
due to the absence of direct data.
6.3.1
Recommendations and Limitations
Regarding the social pillar, no specific method has been used in this study, as Havneholmen
is already constructed. This results in challenges when attempting to take a comprehensive
approach regarding the people living close to the station, especially since the focus is on the
deep construction rather than the metro station itself. During a comprehensive assessment
at the time of the finished structure, the neighbors would likely not be able to distinguish
between the different construction phases, such as which phase produced the most noise.
In relation to noise, Appendix A.1 shows the results from the noise monitoring reports which
lasted over seven months. These reports indicates that the noise levels exceeded the limits
almost all day throughout the monitoring period. The levels remained relatively constant,
suggesting that the type of machinery used did not significantly affect the noise output. Ac-
cording to the tunneling company that conducted the noise reports, the excessive noise levels
did not originate from the construction pit but from the surrounding area. This indicates
that reducing the construction time will not have any effect on the high noise levels. However,
in general high noise levels during the construction phase can lead to community frustra-
tion and reduced social acceptance, making it essential to address these concerns proactively.
To strengthen the social aspect of this study, considering a Social Life Cycle Assessment
(SLCA) could be relevant. An SLCA examines how a project impacts society throughout
its life cycle [34].
Conducting this type of assessment would be particularly valuable in
evaluating the extent of societal involvement in the decision-making process, the effects on
local transport due to the construction, and the economic benefits the station brings to the
community.
To make the social pillar even more robust, it could be beneficial to include the production
of cement and steel in the SLCA, as their production can have significant social implications,
also highlighted in the environmental pillar. In relation to steel, it would also be valuable
to explore how steel production affects working conditions in the factories, as well as how
the transportation of steel to the construction site impacts nearby communities through
increased traffic and potential environmental risks. Implementing an SLCA would require
engaging with stakeholders and collecting relevant data to ensure a comprehensive under-
standing of the social impacts involved.
6.4
Economic Comparison for Budget and Time
A budget has been prepared for both the reference and optimized models, as outlined in
Table 23 and Table 24. It is observed that approximately 3.26 million Danish kroner has
been saved with the optimized design, corresponding to 4.48% of the total budget. While
this indicates some savings with the optimized design, the financial benefits may not be
substantial when viewed in the broader context of the overall project budget.
79/ 83
==PAGE:86==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
It is essential to note that the budget analysis conducted is not sufficiently robust due to
several ambiguities in the budgeting methodology. While the secant piles resulted in a cost
saving of 6%, this figure is subject to variability. The current cost estimates from Spon’s
Price Book are based on concrete volume rather than the number of piles, which introduces
uncertainty. One pile is generally assumed to take one day to install, and therefore, the
primary costs are also influenced by the number of bored piles. This variability can lead to
budget overruns during construction, potentially undermining the project’s financial stabil-
ity.
The anchors yield the highest cost reductions at 44.0%, primarily due to the elimination of
nearly half of the anchors with the optimized design. The removal of the second level of
anchors not only decreases costs but also streamlines the construction process.
The machine operation time obtained from Kronos data in Table 8 indicates that the instal-
lation of anchors is the least time-consuming process. The table also shows that after one
week of anchor installation (out of a total of 13 weeks), the installation of struts begins. This
timeline appears overly optimistic and warrants further investigation into the construction
schedules and processes.
These uncertainties regarding the budget created by Spon’s Price Book highlight the need
for more comprehensive economic analysis techniques.
Furthermore, it is observed that
the timeline in the Kronos database might also lack detail, indicating that a more refined
approach is necessary.
6.4.1
Recommendations and Limitations
Based on the economic considerations analyzed, several methods, such as Life Cycle Cost
Analysis (LCCA), can be employed to deepen the economic understanding of this study, as
described in subsubsection 2.3.3. LCCA is particularly useful for analyzing the total cost
of ownership over the project’s full life cycle and can help make the economic aspect more
robust. However, implementing these tools presents several challenges, such as the necessity
for LCCA to be applied during the early design phase to ensure effectiveness. Obtaining
specific economic data for individual projects remains difficult, as this information is typi-
cally not publicly available.
Additionally, it is crucial to explore elements related to environmental cleanup and regulatory
compliance regarding various types of emissions. This is particularly relevant for the steel
and cement production sectors, which significantly contribute to emissions. Understanding
the production and supply chain of these processes is also pertinent. An analysis of the
costs associated with complying with new regulations for components, such as new materials
used in cement, will highlight the economic implications and underscore the necessity for
sustainable alternative production methods, along with their potential benefits.
In conclusion, while the optimized design shows some cost savings, the economic analysis
must address these uncertainties and incorporate more comprehensive methods to enhance
its financial viability and practical application in construction.
80/ 83
==PAGE:87==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
6.5
Overall performance
For both the soil modeling and the three sustainable pillars, there are several uncertainties
that have been highlighted throughout the report. However, it is assessed that an overall
acceptable performance has been obtained.
The soil modeling was able to achieve acceptable results and also pointed out alternative
ways to conduct the modeling in Plaxis 2D.
The environmental pillar obtained from the LCA shows overall robust results, which have
been compared to the literature and still show justifiable outcomes. The LCA method used
to assess the environmental impact of the deep excavation is acceptable, and although some
uncertainties and assumptions have been made, the overall outcome seems to be robust.
The remaining two pillars seem to be unstable, as no specific and recognized methods have
been used due to limited information. However, it must be noted that, because of this lim-
ited information, these two pillars have been reviewed, and suggested methods have been
highlighted. If the two pillars had been conducted with recognized methods, a sustainability
index would be appropriate to validate the sustainability of the two models through a single
score.
Since two out of the three sustainable pillars are not robust, it would be too uncertain to
create a sustainability index. This uncertainty is, among other factors, due to the lack of
established standards for sustainability indexes, resulting in further assumptions and leading
to a completely unstable index for this project.
On the other hand, the reference model and the optimized model do show some differences. It
is observed that the optimized model might demonstrate some benefits from the soil modeling
and in the three sustainable categories, even though the social and economic pillars are not as
robust and reliable. Among these benefits are reduced wall deflection and thickness, and the
characterization values for the midpoint results obtained from the LCA are generally lower
and more beneficial with the optimized design. However, due to the unstable outcomes, it
will be challenging to compare the two models using a single score.
81/ 83
==PAGE:88==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
7
Conclusion and recommendations
Based on this study, it can be concluded that the optimized design demonstrates some im-
provements in sustainability, however, it falls relative short of Metroselskabet’s requirement
for a 50% reduction in CO2 emissions. This target pertains to the entire operational phase
and not solely to the deep excavation, making it unlikely that the goal will be met. Never-
theless, there remains significant room for progress.
The environmental results indicated that the secant piles were the process contributing the
most significantly to the various environmental impact categories, primarily due to the se-
cant piles large amounts of steel and concrete. This was followed by the base slab and the
struts and beams. Consequently, the piles needed to be optimized, resulting in a reduction
of 180 mm in the thickness of the secant piles, as the thickness was the only dimension that
could be adjusted while the other dimensions remained unchanged.
The soil modeling demonstrated along with the monitoring data that the design of the deep
excavation was conservative, and a more flexible structure could have been implemented. It
was observed that while a more flexible structure might be feasible in terms of wall deflec-
tion, the structural design had reached its limits. Reducing the thickness of the wall even
more would not satisfy the requirements for the secant piles as stipulated by the Eurocode.
Through soil modeling, the optimized design was modified to require only one anchor level
instead of two, and the distance between struts was increased from 6.50 meters to 10.0 me-
ters. However, to maintain the same excavation area, an additional 12 secant piles were
necessary, six of which were secondary piles that included cages.
With this new design it was found that the environmental impact category that decreased
the most with the optimized design was the human carcinogen toxicity, with a reduction of
13.0%, followed by mineral resources, which were reduced by 11.7%. The impact category
that had the least savings was Land Use, which achieved only a 7.10% reduction. To further
reduce the environmental impact, changing the cement type would likely be a more effective
solution.
Ideally, this type of assessment should be conducted concurrently with the project, allowing
for clarification of limited information with the supplier. If any drawings are unclear, the
designer should be involved to provide clarity. Access to the electrical panel on the construc-
tion site would also be essential for a thorough assessment, expanding the system boundary
to encompass more relevant processes.
82/ 83
==PAGE:89==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Another way to optimize the design is to
convert the secant piles from temporary
to permanent structures, which would re-
duce the amount of concrete, steel, ma-
chinery, and time used. In this context,
permanent visible bracing could also be
employed to minimize some of the tem-
porary design elements. This study has
observed that the existing struts are per-
forming well. However, this consideration
relates to the overall design and architec-
ture of the station itself. As shown in Fig-
ure 34, the Gammel Strand Metrostation
features permanent visible bracing, sug-
gesting that this approach is viable.
Figure 34: Gammel Strand Metrostation from
the inside [101].
In relation to changing the secant piles from temporary to permanent, it is essential to ex-
amine why Metroselskabet has adopted a conservative approach of 90.0 mm for the concrete
cover, as Eurocode 2 only mandates 40.0 mm [49]. This also raises questions about which
standards within Eurocode should be revised to allow the use of these designed secant piles
as permanent structures. Additionally, it is important to consider the stringent rules regard-
ing crackwidth for permanent secant piles specified in Eurocode, which is also essential for
evaluating potential reductions in material usage and improving construction efficiency.
Due to the lack of standardized methods for assessing the three pillars of sustainability, it
was determined that the evaluations of the social and economic aspects were insufficient
to draw any meaningful conclusions. Consequently, further research and development in
these areas are necessary to establish a more reliable framework for assessing their impact
on sustainability.
In general, it can be concluded that there is a significant need to focus on sustainable design
solutions in the geotechnical and civil engineering fields. To make progress in this area, it is
essential for these projects to be transparent, enabling future initiatives to use these findings
as a baseline and proceed toward sustainable solutions.

B
LCA results
A description for the characterization at midpoint level is described in subsection B.1.
In subsection B.2 the midpoint method is shown for the first LCA, where subsection B.3
shows the endpoint for the Reference model. subsubsection B.6.1 shows consider 1kg secant
xxxii
==PAGE:122==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
pile when using Endpoint method. Figure B.4 shows the midpoint results for the optimized
model and subsection B.5 shows the endpoint results for the optimized model.
B.1
Characterization at midpoint level
The following expression are from SimaPro database manual - Methods library ([29]) where
the impact categories for the midpoint level is described.
Climate change
The characterization factor of climate change is the global warming potential, based on IPCC
2013 report. For the Individualist perspective 20 year time horizon was used, for Hierarchist
100 years and for Egalitarian 1000 years. Climate-carbon feedbacks are included for non-
CO2 GHGs in the Hierarchist perspective. The unit is yr/kg CO2 equivalents.
Ozone depletion
The characterization factor for ozone layer depletion accounts for the destruction of the
stratospheric ozone layer by anthropogenic emissions of ozone depleting substances (ODS).
The unit is yr/kg CFC-11 equivalents.
Ionizing radiation
The characterization factor of ionizing radiation accounts for the level of exposure for the
global population. The unit is yr/kBq Cobalt-60 equivalents to air.
Fine particulate matter formation
The characterization factor of particulate matter formation is the intake fraction of PM2.5.
The unit is yr/kg PM2.5 equivalents.
Photochemical ozone formation, terrestrial ecosystems
The characterization factor is determined from the change in intake rate of ozone due to
change in emission of precursors (NOx and NMVOC). The unit of ecosystem ozone forma-
tion potential is yr/kg NOx equivalents.
Photochemical ozone formation, human health
The characterization factor is determined from the change in intake rate of ozone due to
change in emission of precursors (NOx and NMVOC). The unit of human health ozone for-
mation potential is yr/kg NOx equivalents.
Terrestrial acidification
The characterization factor for terrestrial acidification is Acidification Potential (AP) de-
rived using the emission weighted world average fate factor of SO2. The unit is yr/kg SO2
equivalents.
Freshwater eutrophication
The characterization factor of freshwater eutrophication accounts for the environmental per-
sistence (fate) of the emission of P containing nutrients. The unit is yr/kg P to freshwater
equivalents.
xxxiii
==PAGE:123==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Marine eutrophication
The characterization factor of marine eutrophication accounts for the environmental per-
sistence (fate) of the emission of N containing nutrients. The unit is yr/kg N to marine
equivalents.
Human toxicity and ecotoxicity
The characterization factor of human toxicity and ecotoxicity accounts for the environmen-
tal persistence (fate) and accumulation in the human food chain (exposure), and toxicity
(effect) of a chemical. The unit is yr/kg 1,4-dichlorobenzeen (1,4-DCB) emitted.
Land use
The amount of land transformed or occupied for a certain time. The unit is m2*yr.
Water use
The factor for the water use is the amount of fresh water consumption. The unit is m3
water consumed. Current implementation includes regionalized characterization factors in
the endpoint version of the method.
Mineral resource scarcity
The characterization factor for mineral resource scarcity is the surplus ore potential. The
unit is kg Copper (Cu) equivalents.
Fossil resource scarcity
The characterization factor of fossil resource scarcity is the fossil fuel potential, based on the
higher heating value. The unit is kg oil equivalents.

D
Reinforcement
In Appendix D the results from the reinforcement is shown, the method is taken directly
from the Detail Design Report, [42]. First the reference model calculations is shown followed
by the optimize model
D.1
Reference model
The flexural and shear reinforcement is shown in Appendix D [104].
It must be noted that for the circular reinforced cross-section, only ’Ref CS1’, which describes
the reference model cross-section 1, contains details about the properties of the circular (or
tubular) concrete cross-section, the stress-strain law at the Ultimate Limit State (ULS), the
calculation of internal forces and moments, and the calculation of cross-section resistance.
However, this is also applicable for the remaining calculations.

H
Sensitivity Analyze
The following tables shows the sensitivity analysis The first table shows the analysis for all
impact categories regarding the base slab (Figure 84), followed by the different steel pro-
cesses in Figure 85, here is the anchor plates and bolts described in relation to the sensitivity
analysis.
Subsequent the sensitivity analysis for the concrete is shown, where Figure 86 illustrated an
overview over the normalized values.
cxlii
==PAGE:232==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Figure 84: Overview over normalization results when considering all relevant process from
the base slab with 1kg.
Anchor Plates
In Figure 33, the three processes called hot-rolled steel shows the least impact across various
categories. This is especially relevant for the anchor plate, which requires specific dimensions
achieved at high temperatures, typically obtained from hot-rolled steel [102]. Appendix H
shows that the three hot-rolled steel processes are more or less the same when looking at
the different impact categories. When looking at the global warming the hot-rolled steel
from Europe without Austria is equal 0.21kgCO2eq, while the hot-rolled steel from RoW
shows a higher impact of 0.30kgCO2 eq. These values are very small indicating a very small
accuracy is needed when choosing between these processes. However, since the process ’hot
rolled-steel, GLO, market for hot rolling, steel’ includes transportation and manufacturing
impacts, it was deemed the best fit for this assessment, given the limited data on the anchor
plates.
Bolts
It’s important to use the correct type of steel, as choosing a steel type like hot-rolled steel for
an incorrect process can significantly affect the result when viewing Figure 33. This is espe-
cially true if a large quantity of steel is involved. When considering the last four categories,
the main difference lies between unalloyed steel and low-alloyed steel. Both steel types can
be used in construction. However, for the bolt connections, low-alloyed steel is necessary, as
many bolts require specific alloy mixtures [103]. Without further information on the used
cxliii
==PAGE:233==
Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
bolts, the low-alloyed, market for steel process is assessed to be valid.
Figure 85: Normalized results for Midpoint (H) with all relevant steel processes are included.

Sustainability analysis for temporary deep excavations
January, 2025
Choice of concrete process to Secant Piles
This sensitivity analysis examines among others the environmental impacts of secant piles
used in construction, as they are the hotspot in the assessment. The goal is to evaluate the
accuracy of the selected concrete processes from the EcoInvent database, specifically within
the compressive strength range of 30-37 MPa. By applying the OAT method, six different
concrete processes are analyzed to identify their impact on the overall life cycle assessment.
The characteristic values from this analysis are shown in Appendix H.
It is observed that the land use category changes the least, with only a 14% variation, while
water consumption varies by 45% across categories. The process with the lowest water con-
sumption is titled ’Concrete, 35MPa GLO — concrete production, 35MPa, with cement,
CEM II/B,’ which also has the lowest value in 15 out of 18 impact categories. The pro-
cess with the highest water consumption is ’Concrete, 30MPa RoW — concrete production,
30MPa, for drilled piles, with Portland cement.’ This higher water consumption is expected,
as it includes both concrete production and requirements to convert concrete into the piles,
which also necessitates water and other processes as machines. According to the Ecoinvent
database, cement for drilled piles also includes ’heat, district or industrial, other than natural
gas RoW — market for heat, district or industrial, other than natural gas,’ which requires
water as well. In contrast, concrete production for 35 MPa GLO does not include any similar
inputs.
To determine what type of process is needed for the final LCA, the normalized midpoint
values are shown in Figure 86 Appendix H. The figure shows that none of the processes
might lead to incorrect outcomes. Therefore, the chosen process for the LCA is the one
for the drilled piles, as it provides a more precise evaluation compared to general concrete
production.
